Witness Liability Without Retraction: Apparent Falsehood
Establishes that witnesses can be liable if their falsehood becomes apparent even without formal retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two cases of witness testimony presented in the text and identify which triggers liability based on the appearance of falsehood.

Core Idea
Witnesses are liable for damages when their false testimony is proven, even if they never retract.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the basis for witness liability is not limited to formal retraction. The author presents a key distinction: testimony of a historical loan versus testimony of a present debt. The difference lies in whether the falsehood becomes apparent, which determines if the witnesses are considered destroyers of the plaintiff's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the text and identify the two scenarios: (1) witnesses testify to a past loan, and (2) witnesses testify to a present debt. Note the outcomes in each case.
The first case involves a loan (iqtirād), the second a general debt (dayn).
2
Determine the ruling for each
State whether the witnesses are liable or not in each case. In the first case they are not liable; in the second they are.
3
Understand the rationale
The text gives the reason: in the first case, falsehood is not certain because the loan could have been discharged later; in the second case, falsehood is certain because they testified to a debt existing at that moment.
The key term is 'zuhūr al-kadhib' – the appearance of falsehood.
4
Apply the principle to related cases
The text extends the logic to blood money: if the person supposedly killed returns alive, the guardian who received blood money is liable unless the witness testified under duress.
This shows the principle applies beyond debt testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن تضمين الشاهد لم ينحصر في رجوعه لما في تلقيح المحبوبي المعبر عنه تارة بفروق الكرابيسي : شهد شاهدان على رجل أن فلاناً أقزضه ألف درهم وقضى القاضي بها ثم أقام المقضي عليه بينة على الدفع قبل القضاء يأمر القاضي برد الألف إليه ولا يضمن الشهود، ولو شهدوا أن له عليه ألف درهم وقضى القاضي بذلك وأخذ الألف ثم أقام المقضى عليه البينة على البراءة قبل القضاء يضمن الشهود ووجه الفرق أن في الوجه الأول لم يظهر كذبهم لجواز أنه أقرضه ثم أبرأه، وفي الوجه الثاني ظهر كذبهم لأنهم شهدوا عليه بالألف في الحال وقد تبين كذبهم فصاروا متلفين عليه ألا ترى أنه لو قال امرأته طالق إن كان لفلان عليه شيء فشهد الشهود أنه أقرضه ألفاً يحكم بالمال ولا يحكم بالوقوع، ولو شهدا أن عليه ألفاً حكم بالمال والوقوع جميعاً تبين بهذا أن الشهادة على الإقراض ليست شهادة على قيام الحق للحال والشهادة بالدين مطلقاً شهادة على الحق في الحال ا هـ . فقد علم تضمينهما بظهور كذبهما من غير رجوع فتضمينهما إذا تيقن كذبهما بالأولى ولذا قال في تلخيص الجامع في باب بطلان الشهادة ؛ أخذ الدية ثم جاء المشهود بقتله حياً ضمن الولي للقبض ظلماً ولا يرجع لسلامة بدله أو الشاهد للإلجاء كمكره ويرجع بما أخذ الولي لملكه ذلك، وكذا لو اقتص لكن عنده إذ ليس للدم مالية تملك بخلاف المدبر ولهذا في عتقه يضمن الشاهد والمكره
Then know that imposing liability on the witness is not confined to his retraction, as in Talqih al-Mahbubi, sometimes expressed as Furūq al-Karābīsī: Two witnesses testified against a man that so-and-so lent him a thousand dirhams, and the judge ruled accordingly; then the judgment debtor established evidence that he paid before the judgment; the judge orders the return of the thousand to him, and the witnesses are not liable. If they testified that he owes him a thousand dirhams, and the judge ruled accordingly and he took the thousand, then the judgment debtor established evidence of discharge before the judgment, the witnesses are liable. The reason for the difference is that in the first case, their falsehood is not apparent because it is possible that he lent him and then discharged him; in the second case, their falsehood is apparent because they testified that he owes him a thousand at the present moment, and their falsehood became clear, so they became destroyers of his property. Do you not see that if he said to his wife 'You are divorced if so-and-so has any claim on him', and the witnesses testify that he lent him a thousand, the judge rules for the money but not for the divorce; but if they testify that he owes him a thousand, he rules for both money and divorce. This shows that testimony of a loan is not testimony of the existence of a right at the moment, while testimony of a debt is testimony of a right at the moment. End. So it is known that they are liable due to the appearance of their falsehood without retraction; then how much more if their falsehood is certain? Therefore it is said in Talkhis al-Jami' in the chapter of invalidation of testimony: He took the blood money, then the person testified as killed came alive. The guardian is liable for the receipt unjustly, and he does not have recourse because the substitute is safe. As for the witness under duress, he has recourse against the guardian for what he took, because the guardian owns it. Similarly if it was retribution, but he has recourse because blood money has no monetary value that can be owned, unlike a mudabbir; so for that reason, in his emancipation, the witness and the one under duress are liable.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage establishes that witnesses can be liable for damages even without formally retracting their testimony, if their falsehood becomes apparent through other evidence. The principle is illustrated by a case from Talqih al-Mahbubi: if witnesses testify to a loan that supposedly occurred in the past, and later evidence shows the debtor paid before the judgment, the witnesses are not liable because the loan could have been made and later discharged. But if they testify to a present debt, and evidence shows the debtor was free from debt at that time, the witnesses are liable because their testimony is about an existing right and it proves false. This shows that liability is based on the actual falsehood and its impact on the judgment. The passage also cites Talkhis al-Jami' for a case where a witness testifies to a killing that later proves false: if the blood money was paid, the witness who testified under duress can recover from the guardian, but if the witness was not under duress, he cannot recover. This further illustrates that liability follows from the harm caused by false testimony.

Key Terms
ظهور الكذب (zuhūr al-kadhib): The emergence of clear evidence that the testimony was false, which triggers liability for the witnesses even without a formal retraction.
التضمين (al-tadmin): Imposing financial liability on the witness due to harm caused by their testimony that is proven false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are witnesses not liable in the first case (testimony of a loan) but liable in the second case (testimony of a present debt)?
A) Because in the first case they did not retract; in the second case they did.
B) Because in the first case the debtor paid before judgment; in the second case after.
C) Because in the first case falsehood is not certain (possible discharge); in the second case falsehood is certain.
D) Because in the first case the plaintiff did not receive the money; in the second case he did.

Key Takeaway
Witnesses are liable for damages caused by their false testimony if the falsehood becomes apparent, even without retraction.

Witness Liability Without Retraction: Loan vs. Debt Testimony
Explaining liability of witnesses when falsehood appears without retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between testimony of a loan and testimony of a debt and apply the Hanafi rule that witness liability arises when falsehood is manifest, even without retraction.

Core Idea
When falsehood is evident, the witness is liable even if he never retracted; only unmanifest falsehood spares him.

The Hanafi school holds a nuanced view on witness liability. It is commonly thought that a witness becomes liable only if he retracts his testimony. However, this lesson shows that liability can arise purely from the appearance of falsehood, regardless of retraction. The key lies in the type of testimony given—whether it affirms a present right or only a past event.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two testimonies
The passage distinguishes shahādat al-iqrāḍ (testimony of a loan) from shahādat al-dayn (testimony of a debt). Note the difference in legal effect.
Loan testimony only affirms a past transaction; debt testimony asserts a present obligation.
2
Contrast the liability outcomes
In the first case (loan), the witnesses are not liable when payment is proven; in the second (debt), they are liable. Ask: why the difference?
3
Analyze the reason (ta'lil)
The author explains: in the loan case, falsehood is not manifest because the loan could have been repaid later. In the debt case, the testimony asserts a present right, so proof of preexisting discharge shows clear falsity.
The rational proof is that the witness becomes like a direct cause of damage (mutlif) when falsehood is evident.
4
Apply to the divorce analogy
Study the conditional divorce example: testimony of loan does not trigger divorce, but testimony of debt does. This reinforces the distinction.
This illustrates the practical consequence of the difference between affirming a past event vs. a present right.
5
Derive the general principle
Conclude that witness liability for tadmin is not restricted to retraction but extends to any case where falsehood becomes manifest.
This is a fortiori: if liability applies when falsehood appears, it certainly applies when falsehood is certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن تضمين الشاهد لم ينحصر في رجوعه لما في تلقيح المحبوبي المعبر عنه تارة بفروق الكرابيسي : شهد شاهدان على رجل أن فلاناً أقرضه ألف درهم وقضى القاضي بها ثم أقام المقضي عليه بينة على الدفع قبل القضاء يأمر القاضي برد الألف إليه ولا يضمن الشهود، ولو شهدوا أن له عليه ألف درهم وقضى القاضي بذلك وأخذ الألف ثم أقام المقضى عليه البينة على البراءة قبل القضاء يضمن الشهود ووجه الفرق أن في الوجه الأول لم يظهر كذبهم لجواز أنه أقرضه ثم أبرأه، وفي الوجه الثاني ظهر كذبهم لأنهم شهدوا عليه بالألف في الحال وقد تبين كذبهم فصاروا متلفين عليه ألا ترى أنه لو قال امرأته طالق إن كان لفلان عليه شيء فشهد الشهود أنه أقرضه ألفاً يحكم بالمال ولا يحكم بالوقوع، ولو شهدا أن عليه ألفاً حكم بالمال والوقوع جميعاً تبين بهذا أن الشهادة على الإقراض ليست شهادة على قيام الحق للحال والشهادة بالدين مطلقاً شهادة على الحق في الحال ا هـ . فقد علم تضمينهما بظهور كذبهما من غير رجوع فتضمينهما إذا تيقن كذبهما بالأولى
Then know that the liability of the witness is not restricted to his retraction, according to what is in Talqih al-Mahbubi, sometimes expressed as Furūq al-Karabisi: Two witnesses testified against a man that so-and-so lent him a thousand dirhams, and the judge decided accordingly; then the defendant produced evidence of payment before the judgment: the judge orders the return of the thousand to him, and the witnesses are not liable. But if they testified that he owes him a thousand dirhams, and the judge decided accordingly, and he took the thousand, then the defendant produced evidence of discharge before the judgment: the witnesses are liable. The difference is that in the first case, their falsehood did not appear, because it is possible that he lent him and then discharged him. In the second case, their falsehood appeared, because they testified to the thousand at the present moment, and their falsehood became evident, so they became those who caused damage to him. Do you not see that if he said, 'My wife is divorced if so-and-so has a claim against him,' and the witnesses testified that he lent him a thousand, the judge rules for the money but does not rule for the occurrence of divorce. If they testified that he owes a thousand, the judge rules for both the money and the occurrence. This shows that testimony of a loan is not testimony of the existence of a right at the present moment, while testimony of a debt absolutely is testimony of a right at the present moment. End. So it is known that they are liable due to the appearance of their falsehood without retraction, and a fortiori when their falsehood is certain.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Furūq al-Karabisi, explaining the liability of witnesses without retraction. The author distinguishes between two types of testimony: testimony of a loan (iqrāḍ) and testimony of a debt (dayn). In the first case, witnesses testify that the defendant borrowed money and the judge orders payment. Later, the defendant proves he paid before judgment. The witnesses are not liable because their testimony does not necessarily imply that the debt existed at the time of testimony—the loan could have been repaid later. In the second case, witnesses testify that the defendant owes a debt immediately (e.g., 'he owes him a thousand now'). When the defendant proves he was already free of the debt before judgment, the witnesses' falsehood is evident, so they are liable as if they caused the loss. The author provides an analogy with a conditional divorce: if someone suspends divorce on the existence of a claim, testimony of a loan does not trigger the divorce because it does not confirm a present right, while testimony of a debt does. The key principle is that liability arises when the falsehood is manifest, even without formal retraction, and certainly when the falsehood is certain.

Key Terms
تضمين (tadmin): Liability of the witness to compensate for the loss caused by his false testimony.
شهادة الإقراض (shahādat al-iqrāḍ): Testimony that a loan transaction occurred in the past, not necessarily that the debt exists now.
شهادة الدين (shahādat al-dayn): Testimony that a debt is currently owed, implying present liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Furūq al-Karabisi, why are the witnesses not liable in the first case (testimony of a loan) even though the payment was made before judgment?
A) Because they retracted their testimony
B) Because their testimony did not imply a present right, so falsehood is not evident
C) Because the judge erred in his ruling
D) Because the defendant waived his right to compensation

Key Takeaway
Witness liability (tadmin) in Hanafi fiqh arises when falsehood is manifest, not only upon retraction, and this is determined by whether the testimony affirms a present right (debt) or only a past event (loan).

Liability in Homicide Cases: Executor vs. Witness
Liability rules for executor and witness in homicide scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when the executor (wali) is liable versus when the witness is liable in homicide cases based on unjust seizure and coercion.

Core Idea
The executor is liable for returning blood money if the victim appears alive; the witness is only liable if coerced.

This passage from Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi' examines a nuanced scenario: after blood money is paid for a supposed homicide, the victim is found alive. Who bears the liability? The answer depends on whether the witness acted freely or under duress, and whether retaliation or pardon occurred. Let's break down the rulings step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario
Consider the case where blood money (diyah) is taken based on testimony that someone was killed, then the alleged victim appears alive. Determine who is primarily liable.
The passage begins with the executor (wali) who received the blood money.
2
Determine liability without coercion
When the witness is not coerced, the executor is liable to return the blood money. He cannot seek recourse from the witness because the victim's safety (the body) remains intact, negating the loss.
The principle: liability falls on the one who unjustly seized property.
3
Apply the exception for coercion
If the witness was coerced (similar to compulsion), the witness becomes liable to indemnify the executor. The executor still returns the blood money but can recover from the witness.
Coercion shifts the liability because the witness caused the loss under duress.
4
Analyze retaliation and pardon
If retaliation (qiṣāṣ) was executed, the witness and coercer are liable for the value of the victim (like a slave) because blood has no monetary value that can be owned. In the case of pardon, there is no liability.
Retaliation is valid but the witness must compensate; pardon extinguishes liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذا قال في تلخيص الجامع في باب بطلان الشهادة ; أخذ الدية ثم جاء المشهود بقتله حياً ضمن الولي للقبض ظلماً ولا يرجع لسلامة بدله أو الشاهد للإلجاء كمكره ويرجع بما أخذ الولي لملكه ذلك، وكذا لو اقتص لكن عنده إذ ليس للدم مالية تملك بخلاف المدبر ولهذا في عتقه يضمن الشاهد والمكره. وفي العفو لا . ولو شهد على الإقرار أو الشهادة ضمن الولي لما مر دون الشاهد لأنه لم يظهر كذبه إذ لا تنافي بخلاف الأول، ولهذا لو ثبت الإبراء ضمن شاهد الدين دون الإقراض. ولو قال إن كان له علي حنث في الأول دون الثاني كما لو وجد المشهود بنكاحها أماً والشاهد عبداً أو مجلوداً في قذف ا.هـ.
And this is stated in Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi' in the chapter on the invalidity of testimony: 'He took the blood money, then the person on whose behalf he was killed appeared alive: the executor (wali) is liable for the unjust seizure, and does not have recourse [to the witness] because the substitute (the body) remains safe. But the witness is liable [to indemnify] the executor due to coercion like compulsion, and he has recourse to what the executor took because the executor owned it. Similarly, if retaliation (qiṣāṣ) occurred, but in his view, blood has no financial value that can be owned, unlike a mudabbar slave; therefore, in his emancipation, the witness and the coercer are liable. And in the case of pardon, there is no liability. And if he testified to an acknowledgment (iqrār) or to a testimony (shahāda), the executor is liable for the reason mentioned, not the witness, because his falsehood did not appear—there is no contradiction [in his testimony] unlike the first case. Therefore, if release (ibrā') is established, the witness of a debt is liable, but not the witness of a loan. And if he said: 'If he has a claim against me,' he incurs the penalty (ḥinth) in the first case but not the second, just as if the woman testified to in marriage is found to be his mother, or the witness is found to be a slave or one who was flogged for slander. End.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi', applying the principle that witnesses are liable when their falsehood is evident, with exceptions. It begins with a case where blood money was taken for a deceased person who later appears alive. The executor (wali) who received the blood money is liable to return it because he seized it unjustly; he cannot recover from the witnesses because the victim's safety (the body) negates the damage. However, if the witnesses were coerced, they are liable to the executor for what he took, as they caused the loss. If retaliation (qiṣāṣ) was carried out, the witnesses are liable for the value of the victim (like a slave) because blood is not property, but the retaliation is valid. Next, if someone testifies to an acknowledgment (iqrār) or another testimony, the falsehood is not evident because the acknowledgment could be true or false; thus the witness is not liable, only the executor who acted on it. The distinction between testimony of a loan and a debt is reiterated: release (ibrā') only makes the witness of a debt liable. The example of a conditional clause: if someone says 'If he has a claim against me,' and witnesses testify to a loan, no penalty occurs; but if they testify to a debt, the penalty occurs. Finally, if a marriage is invalid (e.g., the woman is the man's mother, or the witness is a slave or slanderer), the witness is not liable because their testimony only concerns a past event, and the falsehood is not about a present right.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The executor or guardian who receives blood money.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is deferred until the master's death; used as an analogy for property value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where blood money was taken and the victim appears alive, who is primarily liable to return the money?
A) The witness who testified
B) The executor who received the money
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Liability for blood money falls on the one who unjustly seizes it; coercion can shift liability to the witness.

Liability in Testimony about Rights: Acknowledgment, Loan, and Marriage
Liability of witnesses for testimony about rights and status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a witness is not liable for testimony about past events (acknowledgment, loan, marriage) and apply the principle of 'no contradiction'.

Core Idea
Testimony about past facts (acknowledgment, loan) does not create liability for falsehood because there is no contradiction with a present right.

The passage continues with non-homicide testimonies: testimony to an acknowledgment (iqrār), to a debt vs. loan, and to marriage. The key principle is that the witness's falsehood is not evident because the testimony does not assert a present right. Let's examine these scenarios and their exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand testimony to acknowledgment
When someone testifies that another person acknowledged a debt, and later the acknowledgment is proven false, the witness is not liable. Why? Because the testimony only reports a statement, not the fact of the debt itself.
No contradiction between the testimony and later proof; the acknowledgment could have been false.
2
Distinguish loan vs. debt testimony
If release (ibrā') from a debt is established, the witness of a debt is liable, but not the witness of a loan. The difference: loan testimony asserts a present right (the loan exists), while debt testimony may be about a past obligation.
Loan: present right; debt: can be released, so falsehood is possible.
3
Analyze conditional penalty
If someone says 'If he has a claim against me,' and witnesses testify to a loan, no penalty (ḥinth) occurs because the condition is not fulfilled. But if they testify to a debt, the penalty occurs because the condition is met.
The condition turns on the existence of a claim; loan testimony confirms the claim, debt testimony may not.
4
Apply the marriage example
If a woman is found to be the man's mother, or the witness is a slave or slanderer, the witness is not liable. Their testimony is about past facts (marriage contract), and the falsehood is not evident from the testimony itself.
The disqualification of the witness or the invalidity of the marriage does not make the testimony inherently false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And this is stated in Talkhīṣ al-Jāmi' in the chapter on the invalidity of testimony: 'He took the blood money, then the person on whose behalf he was killed appeared alive: the executor (wali) is liable for the unjust seizure, and does not have recourse [to the witness] because the substitute (the body) remains safe. End.'

It begins with a case where blood money was taken for a deceased person who later appears alive. The executor (wali) who received the blood money is liable to return it because he seized it unjustly; he cannot recover from the witnesses because the victim's safety (the body) negates the damage. إبراء (ibrā'): Release or discharge from a debt.

Witness Liability: Necessary vs. Incidental Statements
Rule on witness liability with clientage example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Ibn al-Shihna's rule on witness liability and apply it to distinguish between necessary and incidental statements in testimony.

Core Idea
Witnesses are liable only for false statements that are essential to the judgment.

This passage presents a critical rule by Ibn al-Shihna: the liability of false witnesses depends on whether their statement was indispensable to the judge's decision. He illustrates this through a complex inheritance case involving clientage. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a witness must compensate for harm caused by false testimony.

Guided Path
1
State the two-part rule
Identify the two conditions: (1) if witnesses mention something necessary for the judgment and it proves false, they are liable; (2) if they mention something unnecessary and it proves false, they are not liable.
The key term is 'lāzim lil-qaḍā'' (necessary for the judgment).
2
Analyze the clientage example
Follow the case: two witnesses say the claimant is the deceased's client and heir (necessary). Judge awards inheritance. Later, another proves different clientage. The false statement was essential, so witnesses are liable.
Note that 'he is his heir' was indispensable; without it, the judge would not have ruled for inheritance.
3
Contrast with the marriage testimony example
The passage alludes to a case where witnesses testify about a marriage but their statement is not essential for the judgment. They would not be liable even if false.
The distinction hinges on whether the statement was needed for the judge to issue the ruling.
4
Derive the underlying legal principle
The rule is based on the causal connection between the false statement and the resulting harm. Liability requires a direct causal link: the judgment would not have occurred without that statement.
This principle is derived through qiyas (analogical reasoning) from cases where liability was imposed or waived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعل لذلك أصلاً العلامة ابن الشحنة في الحكام حيث قال : دقيقة في إيجاب الضمان على الشاهدين الشاهدان متى ما ذكرا شيئاً هو لازم للقضاء ثم ظهر بخلافه ضمنا ومتى ما ذكرا شيئاً لا يحتاج إليه القضاء ثم تبين بخلاف ما قالا لا يضمنان شيئاً حتى إن مولى الموالاة إذا مات وادعى رجل ميراثه بسبب الموالاة فشهد شاهدان أن هذا الرجل مولى هذا الذي أسلم والاه وعاقده وأنه وراثه لا نعلم له وارثاً غيره فقضى له القاضي بميراثه فاستهلكه وهو معسر، ثم إن رجلاً آخر أقام البينة أنه كان نقض الولاء الأول ووالي هذا الثاني وأنه توفي وهذا الثاني مولاه ووراثه لا وارث له غيره فالقاضي يقضي بالميراث للثاني، فيكون الثاني بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الشاهدين الأولين، وإن شاء ضمن المشهود له الأول لأنه ظهر كذب الشاهدين الأولين فيما للحكم به تعلق وبيان ذلك في مسألة الولاء. قولهما هو وارثه لا وارث له غيره أمر لا بد منه للقضاء له بالميراث فإنهم إذا شهدوا بأصل الولاء ولم يقولوا إنه وارثه فالقاضي لا يقضى له بالميراث، وإنما أخذ الأول الميراث بقول الشاهدين الأولين أنه مولاه ووارثه اليوم وقد ظهر كذبهما فضمنا بخلاف مسألة الشهادة بالنكاح فإنهما إذا شهدا أنه مات امرأته لأن قولهما مات وهي امرأته وهي
For this, the scholar Ibn al-Shihna in al-Hukkām made a principle, saying: 'A fine point in imposing liability on the two witnesses: the two witnesses, whenever they mention something that is necessary for the judgment, and then the contrary appears, they are liable. And whenever they mention something that the judgment does not need, and then it becomes clear contrary to what they said, they are not liable at all. For example, the case of the client of clientage (mawla al-muwalāt): if he dies and a man claims his inheritance due to clientage, and two witnesses testify that this man is the client of the deceased, who embraced Islam and entered into a contract of clientage with him, and that he is his heir and they know no other heir for him; the judge rules for him to inherit; he consumes it and is insolvent. Then another man brings evidence that the first clientage was dissolved and he entered into clientage with this second one, and that the deceased died with this second as his client and heir with no other heir. The judge rules for the second to inherit. Then the second has a choice: if he wishes, he can hold the first two witnesses liable, or if he wishes, he can hold the first heir liable, because the falsehood of the first two witnesses appeared concerning what relates to the judgment. The explanation is in the doctrine of clientage. Their saying 'He is his heir, we know no other heir for him' is essential for the judgment of inheritance; if they testify only to the original clientage without saying he is the heir, the judge would not rule for inheritance. The first took the inheritance by the saying of the first two witnesses that he is his client and heir today, and their falsehood appeared, so they are liable. Contrast this with the case of testimony of marriage: if they testify that he died with his wife, because their statement 'he died' and 'she is his wife'...'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational rule by Ibn al-Shihna regarding witness liability. The rule: witnesses are liable only for statements that are indispensable for the judgment (holds the judgment) — i.e., without which the judge could not have issued the verdict. If the statement is merely incidental (e.g., an embellishment), no liability arises from its falsehood. The author illustrates with the complex example of clientage (walā' al-muwālāt). Two witnesses testify that a man is the client and heir of the deceased, knowing no other heir. The judge awards inheritance. Later, another man proves he is the true client. The second heir can hold the first witnesses liable because their statement that the first was the heir was essential for the judgment. However, if witnesses only testify to the marriage (without saying the woman is his wife?), or if they add extra information not needed for the ruling, they are not liable. The distinction is crucial: in marriage testimony, witnesses often testify to a past event (the contract), which does not necessarily require a statement about present status. The rule guards against holding witnesses liable for minor inaccuracies that do not affect the core of the decision.

Key Terms
لازم للقضاء (lāzim lil-qaḍā'): Essential for the judgment; without that statement, the judge could not have issued his ruling.
الموالاة (al-muwālāt): Clientage established by mutual agreement, causing inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Shihna's rule, if two witnesses testify that a man married a woman and that she gave birth to children, and it turns out the marriage was invalid, are they liable for the children?
A) Yes, because their testimony was false
B) No, because the statement about children is not necessary for the judgment of marriage validity
C) Yes, because they caused harm to the woman
D) No, because they retracted

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, witnesses are only liable for false statements that were essential to the judgment, not for incidental inaccuracies.

Introduction to Witness Liability Upon Retraction in Hanafi Law
Outlining the chapter on retraction of testimony and listing categories covered and omitted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general principle of witness liability upon retraction after judgment and identify the categories of liability covered in Kanz al-Daqa'iq and those omitted.

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract after judgment must compensate for the loss they caused, but liability can be dropped in specific circumstances.

This passage opens the chapter on retraction of testimony in the commentary of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author lays down the foundational rule that witnesses who retract after judgment are liable for the value of what was transferred due to their testimony. He then enumerates five cases where this liability falls away and previews the categories of cases he will discuss, noting that some were covered in the base text and others will be explained later. Understanding this structure is essential for mastering the detailed rulings that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the opening sentences to find the principle that witnesses are liable when they retract after judgment. The key word is ضمان (daman).
The rule applies to retraction after judgment, not before.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions that Muhammad differs from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding liability for ghasb (usurpation). Understand the difference: Muhammad excludes ghasb, the majority includes it under itlaf (destruction).
This difference does not affect the general principle but shows the scope of liability.
3
Recognize the five situations where liability drops
The text lists five scenarios in which the witnesses’ liability is extinguished after it was established. Identify each: (1) acknowledgment of dower after payment, (2) acknowledgment of emancipation, (3) gift of the property back, (4) revocation of a gift, (5) inheritance by the defendant.
These are exceptions to the rule.
4
Distinguish covered vs. omitted categories
The author states that the original matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) covered some categories (debt, marriage, sale, etc.) and omitted others (gift, release, etc.). Make a mental list of both sets.
The commentator will explain the omitted categories later.
5
Understand the author’s plan
The commentary will systematically cover each omitted category. This passage serves as a roadmap. Recognize that the structure is pedagogical: introduce the principle, then address special cases.
This is typical of expansion probes in Hanafi fiqh works.

Commentary

Retraction of Witnesses in Gift Cases: Liability and Valuation
Explaining ruling when witnesses retract after testifying to a gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witness liability after retraction in a gift case and apply it to scenarios with changing property value.

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract after judgment are liable for the value of the gift at the time of judgment.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on Al-Kanz, examines a specific case of witness retraction. When witnesses testify to a gift and then retract after the judge has ruled, they become liable for the value of the gifted item. The donor's right to revoke the gift does not exempt them. The valuation is set at the time of judgment, not at the time of testimony or gift. The text contrasts this with a marriage case where retraction does not lead to liability, highlighting that the loss must be tangible property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling: witnesses who testify to a gift (hiba) and possession, then retract after judgment, are liable (wajib) to pay the value of the gifted item.
The ruling is from Al-Muhit, a major Hanafi reference.
2
Identify the legal basis for liability
Understand that the retraction causes the gift to revert, effectively taking the property from the donee. The donor's right to revoke (ruju' fi al-hiba) does not negate this independent cause of loss.
The retraction itself is the actionable harm.
3
Determine the time of valuation
The text states 'اعتبار القيمة يوم القضاء' – valuation is at the date of judgment, not at testimony or gift. This is because the judgment finalizes the transfer.
If the property's value changes, the witnesses pay the higher value if that exists at judgment.
4
Contrast with the marriage case
In the marriage example, witnesses are not liable because marriage does not involve transfer of property; the retraction does not cause a financial loss.
The key distinction: liability attaches only when retraction causes a property loss.
5
Apply to a defective property scenario
If the slave had a defect (white eye) at testimony but lost it by judgment, witnesses pay the value as if cured (higher value), because valuation is at judgment.
This illustrates the rule: value at judgment, not at testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الهبة ففي المحيط شهدوا أنه وهب عبده من فلان وقبضه ثم رجعا بعد القضاء ضمنا قيمة العبد وحق الرجوع لا يمنع التضمين، فإن ضمنهما القيمة لم يرجع فيها لوصول زيادة غير محتاج إليها فإنهما لو قالا كانت امرأته فإن القاضي يقضي لها بالميراث فصار وجود هذه الزيادة والعدم بمنزلة ولو انعدمت هذه الزيادة لكان لا يجب عليهما شيء لأنهما شهدا بنكاح كان ولم يظهر كذبهما في ذلك ثم ذكر مسألة الفروق . مدعي يرجع هـ. العوض ولا . الشاهدان فيها، ولو كان أبيض العين يوم شهدا بالهبة ثم رجعا والبياض زائل ضمنا قيمته أبيض لاعتبار القيمة يوم القضاء اهـ.
As for gift, in Al-Muhit: They testified that he gave his slave as a gift to so-and-so and he took possession, then they retracted after judgment: they are liable for the value of the slave, and the right of revocation does not prevent liability. If they are made liable for its value, they do not take it back because a surplus has accrued that is not needed. If they had said she was his wife, the judge would give her the inheritance, so this surplus and its absence are equivalent, and if this surplus were absent, nothing would be obligatory on them because they testified to a marriage that existed and their falsehood did not appear in that. Then he mentioned the issue of differences. The claimant returns? The compensation, no? The two witnesses in it, and if the slave was white-eyed when they testified to the gift, then they retract and the whiteness has gone, they are liable for its value as white, considering the value on the day of judgment.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for witnesses who retract after testifying to a gift (hiba). According to Al-Muhit, if witnesses testify that a person gave a slave as a gift and the recipient took possession, then the witnesses retract after judgment, they are liable for the value of the slave. The donor's right to revoke the gift (ruju' fi al-hiba) does not prevent this liability because the retraction caused the property to be taken away from the donor. If the witnesses are made to pay the value, they cannot subsequently reclaim the payment even if some condition changes, because the property has been transferred irrevocably. A contrasting case is given: if witnesses testify to a marriage, and then retract, they are not liable because the marriage itself is a valid contract and the testimony did not cause a transfer of property (only marital rights). For gift, the loss is clear. If the slave had a defect (white eye) at the time of testimony but lost it by the time of judgment, the witnesses are liable for the higher value (as white) because valuation is at the time of judgment.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift; a contract of donation that requires offer, acceptance, and delivery of possession.
رجوع في الهبة (ruju' fi al-hiba): The donor's right to revoke the gift, which exists before possession or under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At the time of the gift
B) At the time of testimony
C) At the time of retraction
D) At the time of judgment

Key Takeaway
Witnesses who retract after testifying to a gift are liable for its value at the time of judgment, and the donor's right to revoke does not protect them.

Retraction in Cases of Release (Ibra') and Deferment (Ta'jil)
Explaining witness liability upon retraction in ibra'/ta'jil cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on witness liability when they retract after testifying to ibra' or ta'jil, and apply the exceptions regarding debtor's insolvency and remission of the term.

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract after testifying to ibra' or ta'jil must pay the debt — but not if the debtor dies insolvent.

This passage from Al-Muhit and Al-Utabiyya examines a practical scenario in court testimony. When witnesses change their testimony after a judgment based on their first testimony, liability arises. The Hanafi school's position is detailed across several variations, including the impact of deferment and insolvency. Understanding these nuances is crucial for fiqh of testimony (shahadat) and for muftis dealing with retraction cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that witnesses who testify to ibra' or ta'jil and then retract are liable (damana) for the debt.
2
Recognize temporal variations
Liability applies whether retraction occurs before or after the deferred term expires.
3
Understand the witnesses' right of recourse
If they pay, they can seek reimbursement from the debtor up to the original term.
4
Note the exception for insolvency
If the debtor dies insolvent before retraction, witnesses are not liable because no loss occurred.
5
Apply to conflicting testimony
When two pairs testify (one for debt, one for release), the retracting release witnesses are liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإبراء والتأجيل ففي المحيط : شهدا أنه أبرأه عن الدين أو أجله سنة أو أوفاه فقضى به ثم رجعا ضمنا، ولو شهدا أنه أجله سنة فقضى بها ثم رجعا قبل الخلول أو بعده ضمنا ورجعا به على المطلوب إلى أجله ويبرأ الشاهدان بقبض الطالب الدين بعد مضي الأجل من المطلوب، فإن ضمنا رجعا به على المطلوب إلى أجله وقاما مقام الطالب فإن نوى ما على المطلوب فمن مالهما، ولو أسقط المديون الأجل لم يضمنا ولو شهدا أن له على آخر ألفاً وآخران أنه أبرأه ثم رجعوا كلف الألف إقامة البيئة ثانياً وخصمه في دلك شهود براءة الدين رجعوا فيضمنها الألف، ولا تصح إقامة البينة على الدين إلا بحضرة الشهود لا بحضرة المدعى عليه ولا يرجعان على المشهود له بالبراءة ا وفي العتابية : شهدوا على أنه أبرأه من الديون ثم مات الغريم مفلساً ثم رجعا لم يضمنا للطالب لأنه توى ما عليه بالإفلاس ا هـ
As for release (ibra') and deferment (ta'jil), in Al-Muhit: If they testified that he released him from the debt or deferred it for a year or paid him, and judgment was passed accordingly, then they retracted, they are liable. And if they testified that he deferred it for a year and judgment was passed, then they retracted before or after the term expires, they are liable and they can recover the amount from the debtor up to his term, and the two witnesses are absolved when the creditor receives payment after the term from the debtor. If they are made to pay, they can recover from the debtor up to his term and stand in the place of the creditor. If the debtor wills what is on him, then it is from his wealth. And if the debtor remits the term, they are not liable. And if they testify that he has a thousand on another and two others testify that he released him, then they retract, the one who claimed the thousand must establish evidence again, and his opponent is the witnesses to release who retract, so they are liable for the thousand. And it is not valid to establish evidence on the debt unless in the presence of the witnesses, not in the presence of the defendant, nor can they recover from the one who was witness to the release. And in Al-Utabiyya: They testified that he released him from debts, then the debtor died insolvent, then they retract: they are not liable to the creditor because what was on him is lost due to insolvency.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage covers retraction in cases of ibra' (release of debt) and ta'jil (deferment of payment). According to Al-Muhit, if witnesses testify that the creditor released the debtor from the debt, or deferred it, or paid it, and the judge passes judgment accordingly, then the witnesses retract, they are liable to the creditor for the amount of the debt. The timing of retraction (before or after the deferred term) does not affect liability; they are liable in either case. However, if they pay, they can seek reimbursement from the debtor up to the original term. If the creditor later receives payment from the debtor after the term, the witnesses are discharged. If the debtor voluntarily remits the deferred term (i.e., allows early payment), the witnesses are not liable. An interesting case: if two pairs of witnesses testify, one group for the debt and another for release, and then the release witnesses retract, they are liable to the creditor. The creditor must re-prove the debt in the presence of the witnesses (not the defendant). If the debtor dies insolvent after the testimony, and then the witnesses retract, they are not liable because the debt was already uncollectible.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Release from a debt; a unilateral discharge of obligation by the creditor.
تأجيل (ta'jil): Deferment of payment of a debt to a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if witnesses testify that a creditor deferred a debt for a year, judgment is passed, then the witnesses retract after the year has passed, what is the ruling?
A) They are not liable because the term has ended
B) They are liable and must pay the debt amount
C) They are liable but only for half the debt
D) They are liable only if the debtor paid the creditor

Key Takeaway
Witnesses who retract after testifying to ibra' or ta'jil must pay the debt, but not if the debtor dies insolvent before retraction.

No Liability for Witnesses Retracting in Lineage Cases
Ruling on witness liability in lineage cases with rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh holds that witnesses who retract after establishing lineage are not liable for property compensation, citing the reasoning that lineage is not property and inheritance is attributed to death.

Core Idea
Witnesses to lineage face no property liability because lineage is not property and inheritance is caused by death, not testimony.

This passage from Al-Walwalijiyya addresses a specific scenario: a man claims to be another's son, the father denies, but witnesses establish the lineage. If the witnesses later retract, are they liable for any inheritance the son may have received? The Hanafi ruling is that they are not. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this seemingly counterintuitive rule, which hinges on the distinction between property rights and personal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Ruling
First, understand the facts: a man claims to be another's son, the father denies, witnesses testify, lineage is established, then witnesses retract. The ruling: no liability (la daman) on the witnesses.
The ruling applies whether retraction occurs during the father's life or after his death.
2
Locate the Evidence (Dalil)
The evidence is a rational proof (dalil aqli): lineage is not property, so it cannot be the basis for property liability. This is a type of qiyas on the general principle that financial liability only attaches to financial rights.
No direct Quran or Hadith is cited; the reasoning is based on the nature of lineage.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (How the Proof Applies)
During the father's life, the witnesses testified only about lineage, which is not property. After death, inheritance is caused by the father's death, not by the testimony. Even if the testimony contributed to establishing the son's right, the loss of inheritance to other heirs is not directly compensable.
The text breaks off mid-sentence, but the complete reasoning is clear from the part given.
4
Note the Practical Implication (Thamarah)
The practical result: witnesses in lineage cases are protected from financial liability, encouraging testimony in family law matters. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that only property or rights with monetary value are compensable.
This is a contrast with cases where witnesses testify about financial matters and retract—they may be liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحد فسنذكره مع القصاص، وأما النسب والولاء والكتابة وأخواها فمع العتق أي فسنذكرها مع العتق الآتي في كلام المتن والمراد بأخوي الكتابة التدبير والاستيلاد وكأنه رحمه الله تعالى نسي فلم يذكر شيئاً من أحكام النسب والولاء مستقلاً بل ذكر الثلاثة فقط، ولعله اكتفاء بما تضمنته من الولاء والنسب . وفي الولوالجية: ولو ادعى رجل أنه ابن رجل والأب يجحد وأقام البينة أنه ابنه ولد على فراشه فقضي بذلك وأثبت نسبه ثم رجعوا فلا ضمان عليهم سواء رجعوا في حال حياة الأب أو بعد وفاته، أما في حال حياة الأب فلأنهما لم يشهدا على الأب بالمال وإنما شهدا عليه بالنسب والنسب ليس بمال وما ليس بمال لا يضمن بالمال، وأما بعد وفاته فلأنهم لو ضمنوا ما ورث الابن المشهود له لسائر الورثة ولا يجوز ذلك لأن استحقاق الميراث يضاف إلى موت الأب لا إلى النسب لأن الميراث يستحق
As for the fixed punishment (hadd), we will mention it with retaliation (qisas). As for lineage (nasab), clientage (wala'), kitaba, and their siblings, they are with emancipation ('itaq), i.e., we will mention them with the upcoming emancipation in the text. And by the siblings of kitaba are meant tadbir and istilad. And it is as if the author (may Allah have mercy on him) forgot, for he did not mention any rulings of nasab and wala' independently, but only mentioned the three, perhaps contenting himself with what is included of wala' and nasab. And in Al-Walwalijiyya: If a man claims to be the son of a man, and the father denies, and he establishes evidence that he is his son born on his bed, and judgment is given accordingly, and his lineage is established, then the witnesses retract: there is no liability on them, whether they retract during the father's life or after his death. As for during the father's life, because they did not testify against the father for property; they only testified about lineage, and lineage is not property, and what is not property is not compensated with property. As for after his death, because if they were held liable for what the son as witness inherited from other heirs, that would not be valid because the entitlement to inheritance is attributed to the father's death, not to lineage, because inheritance is due...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage first provides cross-references for categories not yet discussed: hadd (fixed punishment) will be covered with qisas (retaliation); nasab (lineage), wala' (clientage), and kitaba (contract of emancipation) along with tadbir and istilad will be covered with 'itaq (emancipation). The author notes that the original text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not independently discuss nasab and wala' rulings, but he will cover them within the section on emancipation. Then, from Al-Walwalijiyya, a specific ruling is given: witnesses who testify to a man's lineage (e.g., claiming he is the son of a certain father), and judgment establishes the lineage, then the witnesses retract — they are not liable for any property compensation. This applies whether retraction occurs during the father's lifetime or after his death. The reasoning: during the father's life, testimony was only about lineage, which is not property; after death, even though inheritance rights follow from lineage, the loss of inheritance is not directly caused by the witnesses' testimony but by the father's death. The text ends mid-sentence, explaining that inheritance entitlement attaches to death, not lineage.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage; the legal relationship of paternity or descent.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the relationship between a freed slave and his emancipator, which has inheritance implications.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation; the obligation to make good a loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from Al-Walwalijiyya, what is the reasoning for no liability on witnesses who retract after the father's death?
A) The witnesses did not testify about property during the father's life.
B) Inheritance is attributed to the father's death, not to the testimony.
C) The retraction happened after the father's death, so it is too late.
D) The father's denial invalidated the testimony.

Key Takeaway
Witnesses who retract after establishing lineage are not liable for property compensation because lineage is not property and inheritance is caused by death, not testimony.

Retraction of Witnesses in Agency Cases
Explaining liability when witnesses to agency retract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that witnesses to agency are not liable upon retraction and apply the principle of direct causation (mubashir) vs. indirect cause (sabab) to determine liability in similar cases.

Core Idea
Witnesses to agency are not liable because liability follows the direct actor, not the indirect cause.

This passage from Al-Muhit and Al-Utabiyya addresses a specific scenario: witnesses testify that a person appointed an agent to collect a debt, the agent collects, the principal denies agency, and the witnesses then retract. Are the witnesses liable? The Hanafi answer is no, based on the fundamental distinction between direct causation (mubashir) and indirect causation (sabab). Understanding this ruling helps clarify the broader Hanafi principle of liability in tort.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage carefully. Two witnesses testify that X appointed Y as agent to collect a debt from Z. Y collects. X denies agency. The witnesses then retract. What is the legal issue?
The key is to isolate the action (retraction) and its effect on liability.
2
State the ruling from the text
According to Al-Muhit and Al-Utabiyya, the witnesses are not liable (la yadmanna). Confirm this ruling.
The passage explicitly says 'lam yadumna'.
3
Identify the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text explains that the witness is a cause (sabab) for removing the possibility of collection, but the agent is the direct actor (mubashir). Liability is on the direct actor.
This is a qiyas: compare to a standard agency where the agent is liable for his direct actions.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The asl is the general principle that the direct actor bears liability. The far is the witnesses' testimony. The illa (effective cause) is that the witnesses are only an indirect cause, while the agent directly executed the collection.
Note: The principal is not liable either because he did not act; the agent acted.
5
Note the authority sources
The ruling is taken from Al-Muhit and Al-Utabiyya, which are later fatwa collections (fatawa mutakhkhirin). This indicates the weight of the ruling.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوكالة ففي المحيط : شهدا أنه وكله بقبض دينه من فلان أو وديعته فقبضه وأنكر الموكل ثم رجعا لم يضمنا لأن الشاهد سبب لتفويت إمكان القبض على الموكل والوكيل باشر تفويته فيكون الضمان على المباشر وفي العتابية ولا ضمان على شهود التوكيل بالإعتاق ولا على شهود التفويض ولا على شهود التوكيل بقبض الدين ا هـ
As for agency (wakala), in Al-Muhit: If they testified that he appointed him as agent to collect his debt from so-and-so or his deposit, and he collected it, and the principal denies, then they retract: they are not liable because the witness is a cause for removing the possibility of collection from the principal, but the agent directly caused the removal, so liability is on the direct actor. And in Al-Utabiyya: There is no liability on witnesses to agency for emancipation, nor on witnesses to delegation, nor on witnesses to agency for debt collection.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling when witnesses retract after testifying to the appointment of an agent (wakala). According to Al-Muhit, if witnesses testify that a person appointed an agent to collect a debt or deposit, and the agent actually collected the money, then the principal denies the agency, and the witnesses retract, they are not liable. The reasoning: the witness is only an indirect cause (sabab) of the loss of the right to collect, but the agent is the direct cause (mubashir) of the actual collection. In Hanafi tort law, liability falls on the direct actor, not the indirect cause. Therefore, the witnesses are not liable. Al-Utabiyya confirms this, adding that witnesses to agency for emancipation, delegation, or debt collection are not liable. This distinguishes agency from cases like gift or ibra' where the witnesses themselves are the direct cause of the legal transfer.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakala): Agency contract where one person appoints another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
مباشر (mubashir): Direct actor; the person who directly performs an action causing loss.
سبب (sabab): Indirect cause; a person whose action contributes to a loss but is not the direct actor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why are witnesses to an agency appointment not liable upon retraction after the agent has collected the debt?
A) Because the debt was already paid
B) Because the agent is the direct actor and bears liability
C) Because the witnesses did not testify about the debt itself
D) Because the principal consented to the agency

Key Takeaway
Witnesses to agency are not liable upon retraction because liability follows the direct actor (mubashir), not the indirect cause (sabab).

Liability of Retracting Pledge Witnesses
Liability of retracting pledge witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of witnesses who retract from testifying to a pledge (rahn) under different conditions (surplus, death of property, denial by pledgee).

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract a pledge testimony are liable only for losses they cause: surplus if property perishes, debt if security is lost.

In pledge (rahn) transactions, witnesses may later retract their testimony. This lesson from Al-Muhit clarifies when they become liable for compensation. The key is whether their retraction causes harm to the pledgor or pledgee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario type
Determine the exact claim: is the property worth exactly the debt or has a surplus? Who admits what?
The text distinguishes cases where the debtor admits the debt and the pledgee claims the pledge, and where the pledgor claims the pledge but pledgee denies.
2
Check the condition of the pledged property
Is the property alive (still in existence) or did it perish? Liability for surplus only arises if the property dies while in the pledgee's possession.
If the property is alive, no liability for surplus regardless of retraction.
3
Assess the nature of retraction
Did the witnesses retract the entire testimony (pleading ignorance) or only the delivery (saying it was delivered but not as pledge)?
Retracting only delivery absolves them completely.
4
Apply the liability rule
Match the scenario to the rule: if surplus and property perished, liable for surplus; if pledgee denies, liable for debt; otherwise no liability.
Liability is conditional on causation of loss.
5
Note the exception
Retraction regarding delivery without retracting the pledge itself carries no liability.
This is a specific exception from the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرهن ففي المحيط : ادعى من له ألف على آخر أنه رهنه عبداً بها قيمته ألف والمطلوب مقر بالدين وشهدا بالرهن ثم رجعا لم يضمنا لأنهما أزالا بعوض، ولو كان فيه فضل على الدين لم يضمنا ما دام العبد حياً، فإن مات في يد المرتهن ضمنا الفضل على الدين ولو ادعى الراهن الرهن وأنكر المرتهن لم يضمنا الفضل ويضمنان قدر الدين للمرتهن وإن رجعا عن الرهن دون التسليم بأن قالا سلم إليه هذا العبد وما رهنه لا يضمنان اهـ
As for pledge (rahn), in Al-Muhit: A person who has a thousand owed to him by another claims that the debtor pledged a slave to him worth a thousand, and the debtor admits the debt, and they testify to the pledge, then they retract: they are not liable because they removed it for compensation. And if there was a surplus above the debt, they are not liable as long as the slave is alive; if he dies in the possession of the pledgee, they are liable for the surplus above the debt. And if the pledgor claims the pledge and the pledgee denies, they are not liable for the surplus, but they are liable for the amount of the debt to the pledgee. And if they retract from the pledge without the delivery, by saying 'he delivered to him this slave and did not pledge it', they are not liable.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage covers the liability of witnesses who testify to a pledge (rahn) and then retract. According to Al-Muhit, if a creditor claims a debtor pledged a slave worth the exact debt amount, the debtor admits the debt, and witnesses testify to the pledge, then retract, they are not liable because their testimony resulted in the creditor obtaining a security interest (compensation for the debt). However, if the slave's value exceeds the debt, and the slave dies in the pledgee's possession, the witnesses are liable for the surplus value (since the debtor lost that surplus). If the pledgor claims the pledge but the pledgee denies (i.e., the pledgee does not admit possession), the witnesses are liable for the full debt amount to the pledgee (because the pledgee loses his security). If the witnesses retract regarding the delivery of the slave but not the pledge itself (e.g., they say the slave was delivered but not as a pledge), they are not liable at all.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge; a security interest in property held by a creditor until debt repayment.
فضلة (fadla): Surplus value of the pledged property above the debt amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A witness testifies that a slave worth exactly the debt amount (no surplus) was pledged. They retract. The slave dies in the pledgee's possession. What is the witness's liability?
A) The full value of the slave
B) The debt amount
C) Nothing
D) The surplus

Key Takeaway
In pledge witness retraction, liability is not automatic but depends on whether the retraction caused a financial loss and its nature (surplus vs security).

Beginning of the ruling on retraction in lease cases, incomplete

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A lease contract is binding; its retraction depends on the stage of the contract.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq begins the discussion of retraction in lease (ijara) cases. Although the text is incomplete, the opening example from Al-Muhit—a man riding another's camel to Mecca—is a classic Hanafi scenario for a lease of conveyance. In this lesson, we will use this opening to establish the foundational Hanafi view on the binding nature of ijara.

Guided Path
1
Locate the example in Al-Muhit

2
Understand the hypothetical
This is a hypothetical scenario to illustrate a legal issue. The journey to Mecca is a common example for a lease of conveyance (ijara al-muta'addi). In Hanafi fiqh, such a lease is valid if the term and fee are specified.
The legal issue likely revolves around retraction (ruju')—either the parties retracting the contract or witnesses retracting testimony about it.
3
Note the incomplete nature
The passage cuts off after stating the example. The full ruling is on the next page. However, we can already infer that the Hanafi view is that ijara is a binding contract (lazim). Retraction is generally not allowed without mutual consent or a valid legal reason (e.g., defect, fraud).
The subsequent pages would elaborate on the consequences of retraction by witnesses or parties, which is a topic in the chapter on testimony (shahadat).

Liability of Witnesses Upon Retraction in Ibra' and Ta'jil
Rulings on liability when witnesses retract testimony about remission or postponement of debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which retracting witnesses are liable for compensation in cases of remission or postponement of debt, and apply those conditions to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract after judgment are liable unless the loss is caused by something else like insolvency.

This passage from al-Muhit and al-Utabiyya deals with a specific scenario: witnesses testify about remission or postponement of a debt, the judge rules on that basis, and then the witnesses retract. The central question is whether they must compensate the plaintiff for the lost debt. The ruling draws a careful distinction between loss caused by the retraction and loss caused by an independent factor like the debtor's insolvency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
List the different situations described: testimony of remission, postponement, payment; multiple sets of witnesses; debtor's insolvency.
2
State the general principle
Extract the rule: if witnesses testify, judgment is given, then they retract, they are liable (daman).
Applicable to ibra' and ta'jil.
3
Recognize the exception
Identify the exception: if the debtor dies insolvent, witnesses are not liable because the loss is due to insolvency, not retraction.
From al-Utabiyya.
4
Understand recourse
Explain that if witnesses pay, they can recover from the original debtor within the original term, standing in the plaintiff's place.
5
Apply to multi-witness scenario
Work through the case where one set testifies for debt and another for remission, then both retract. The remitting witnesses become liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإبراء والتأجيل ففي المحيط : شهدا أنه أبرأه عن الدين أو أجله سنة أو أوفاه فقضى به ثم رجعا ضمنا، ولو شهدا أنه أجله سنة فقضى بها ثم رجعا قبل الخلول أو بعده ضمنا ورجعا به على المطلوب إلى أجله ويبرأ الشاهدان بقبض الطالب الدين بعد مضي الأجل من المطلوب، فإن ضمنا رجعا به على المطلوب إلى أجله وقاما مقام الطالب فإن نوى ما على المطلوب فمن مالهما، ولو أسقط المديون الأجل لم يضمنا ولو شهدا أن له على آخر ألفاً وآخران أنه أبرأه ثم رجعوا كلف الألف إقامة البيئة ثانياً وخصمه في دلك شهود براءة الدين رجعوا فيضمنها الألف، ولا تصح إقامة البينة على الدين إلا بحضرة الشهود لا بحضرة المدعى عليه ولا يرجعان على المشهود له بالبراءة ا وفي العتابية : شهدوا على أنه أبرأه من الديون ثم مات الغريم مفلساً ثم رجعا لم يضمنا للطالب لأنه توى ما عليه بالإفلاس ا هـ
As for remission and postponement, in al-Muhit: If two witnesses testified that he remitted a debt or postponed it for a year or paid him, and the judge ruled accordingly, then they retracted, they are liable. If they testified that he postponed it for a year and the judge ruled accordingly, then they retracted before or after the term, they are liable and they have recourse against the defendant for the amount up to its term, and the two witnesses become free from liability upon the plaintiff collecting the debt from the defendant after the term. If they paid (the liability), they have recourse against the defendant for the amount up to its term, standing in the place of the plaintiff. If the debtor foregoes the term, they are not liable. If they testified that someone has a thousand against another, and two others testified that he remitted it, then they all retracted, the one who owes the thousand is required to produce evidence a second time, and his opponent in that is the witnesses of the remission of debt; if they retracted, they become liable for the thousand. It is not valid to present evidence for a debt except in the presence of the witnesses, not in the presence of the defendant. And they (the testifiers of remission) do not have recourse against the one for whom remission was testified. And in al-Utabiyya: They testified that he remitted debts from him, then the debtor died insolvent, then they retracted; they are not liable to the plaintiff because his right was lost due to insolvency.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit and al-Utabiyya, transmitted in the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, presents rulings on retraction of testimony regarding remission (ibra') and postponement (ta'jil) of debts. The general principle is that if witnesses testify to a fact that leads to a judgment and then retract, they are liable for the amount that was lost due to their testimony. However, if the debtor dies insolvent, the witnesses are not liable because the loss is due to insolvency, not their testimony. The passage also details the process of recourse: if the witnesses pay the debt, they can recover from the original debtor within the original term. If the debtor foregoes the term, the witnesses are not liable. A special case involves multiple sets of witnesses: if one set testifies for the debt and another for remission, and both retract, the debt is reinstated and the remitting witnesses are liable. Evidence for the debt must be presented again in the presence of the witnesses. The ruling in al-Utabiyya adds that if the debtor dies insolvent after the remission testimony, the retracting witnesses are not liable because the right was already lost.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Remission of debt; a legal act cancelling the obligation without payment.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): Postponement of the due date of a debt.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability; here it means the witnesses must pay the amount lost due to their retracted testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not liable because the term has passed.
B) They are liable and must pay the debt immediately.
C) They are liable but can recover from the debtor only after the new term.
D) They are liable only if the creditor sues them.

Key Takeaway
Retracting witnesses after judgment are liable for compensation unless the loss is independently caused, such as by debtor insolvency.

Witness Liability for Retracted Testimony on Agency
Ruling on witness liability for retracted testimony in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why witnesses who retract testimony about agency are not liable under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
The witness is a cause (sabab); the agent is the direct actor (mubashir); liability falls on the direct actor.

When witnesses testify that someone was appointed as an agent to collect a debt or deposit, and the agent collects while the principal denies the agency, the witnesses may later retract. The Hanafi ruling is clear: they are not liable. This lesson unpacks the distinction between cause and direct action, and extends the principle to similar agency scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is retraction of testimony regarding agency (wakala). The ruling is that the witnesses are not liable (la daman).
2
Understand the parties involved
There are three parties: the principal (muwakkil), the agent (wakil), and the witnesses (shuhud). The principal denies the agency; the agent collects the debt; the witnesses retract.
3
Distinguish cause (sabab) from direct action (mubashar)
The witness's testimony is a cause for the loss of the principal's ability to collect, but the agent directly performed the collection. In Hanafi tort law, liability is on the direct actor (mubashir), not on the one who provided a cause (sabab).
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: al-daman 'ala al-mubashir la 'ala al-sabab.
4
Extend to other agency scenarios
The same applies to agency for emancipation (i'taq), delegation (tafwid), or debt collection. In all these, the witness is a cause, so no liability.
Al-Utabiyya confirms no liability on witnesses of agency for emancipation, delegation, or debt collection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوكالة ففي المحيط : شهدا أنه وكله بقبض دينه من فلان أو وديعته فقبضه وأنكر الموكل ثم رجعا لم يضمنا لأن الشاهد سبب لتفويت إمكان القبض على الموكل والوكيل باشر تفويته فيكون الضمان على المباشر وفي العتابية ولا ضمان على شهود التوكيل بالإعتاق ولا على شهود التفويض ولا على شهود التوكيل بقبض الدين ا هـ
As for agency, in al-Muhit: If they testified that he appointed him as agent to collect his debt from so-and-so or his deposit, and he collected it while the principal denied, then they retracted, they are not liable because the witness is a cause for losing the possibility of collection for the principal, while the agent directly caused the loss; thus liability is on the direct doer. And in al-Utabiyya: There is no liability on witnesses of agency for emancipation, nor on witnesses of delegation, nor on witnesses of agency for debt collection.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit and al-Utabiyya addresses retraction of testimony regarding agency (wakala). The ruling is that witnesses who testify that someone was appointed as an agent to collect a debt or deposit, and the agent collects it while the principal denies the agency, then the witnesses retract—they are not liable. The reason is that the witness's testimony was merely a cause (sabab) for the loss of the possibility of the principal collecting, but the agent (the direct actor) is the one who actually took the money. Liability is on the direct perpetrator (mubashir), not the cause. Al-Utabiyya extends this: witnesses of agency for emancipation (i'taq), delegation (tafwid), or agency for debt collection are also not liable.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakala): Agency; a contract where one person appoints another to act on his behalf.
المباشر (al-mubashir): The direct doer; the one who physically performs the act causing loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because their testimony enabled Y to collect.
B) No, because the witness is only a cause while Y is the direct actor.
C) Yes, but only half the amount.
D) No, because X admitted the agency.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a witness who retracts testimony about agency is not liable because the agent is the direct actor; the witness is merely a cause.

Liability for Retracting Testimony on Pawn
Ruling on witness liability for retracting pawn testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of witnesses who retract testimony about a pawn transaction under different scenarios, including when the pawned property perishes.

Core Idea
Witnesses retracting pawn testimony are not liable for the property unless it perishes with excess value over the debt.

When witnesses testify to a pawn and later retract, their liability depends on the status of the pawned item and the presence of excess value. This ruling from al-Muhit clarifies that the default is no liability because the pawn is made in exchange for a debt. However, liability arises if the pawned item perishes and its value exceeds the debt, as the witnesses' testimony caused the pledgee to incur the loss of the excess.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
Start with the case where a creditor claims a pawn, the debtor admits the debt, two witnesses testify to the pawn, then retract.
This is the default situation.
2
Apply the default rule
The witnesses are not liable because they removed the pawn in exchange for consideration — the debt remains owed.
Consideration means the pawn secures a debt.
3
Check for excess value
If the pawned item has value exceeding the debt (fadl), the witnesses are still not liable as long as the item remains alive.
Liability only triggers upon destruction.
4
Apply condition of destruction
If the pawned item dies in the pledgee's hands, the witnesses become liable for the excess amount.
This is because their testimony enabled possession leading to loss.
5
Consider denial or retraction without pawn
If the pledgee denies the pawn, the witnesses are not liable for the excess but are liable for the debt amount to the pledgee. If they retract the pawn but affirm delivery, they are not liable.
Different fact patterns yield different liabilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرهن ففي المحيط : ادعى من له ألف على آخر أنه رهنه عبداً بها قيمته ألف والمطلوب مقر بالدين وشهدا بالرهن ثم رجعا لم يضمنا لأنهما أزالا بعوض، ولو كان فيه فضل على الدين لم يضمنا ما دام العبد حياً، فإن مات في يد المرتهن ضمنا الفضل على الدين ولو ادعى الراهن الرهن وأنكر المرتهن لم يضمنا الفضل ويضمنان قدر الدين للمرتهن وإن رجعا عن الرهن دون التسليم بأن قالا سلم إليه هذا العبد وما رهنه لا يضمنان اهـ
As for pawn, in al-Muhit: If someone who has a thousand against another claims that he pawned a slave worth a thousand with him, and the defendant admits the debt, and two witnesses testify to the pawn, then they retract, they are not liable because they removed (the pawn) in exchange for consideration. If there was excess over the debt, they are not liable as long as the slave is alive. But if the slave dies in the hands of the pledgee, they are liable for the excess over the debt. If the pawner claims the pawn and the pledgee denies, they are not liable for the excess, but they are liable for the amount of the debt to the pledgee. If they retract regarding the pawn without delivery, by saying 'he delivered this slave to him and he did not pawn it', they are not liable.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit provides rulings for retraction of testimony about pawn (rahn). The general rule: if witnesses testify to a valid pawn and then retract, they are not liable for the pawned item itself because the pawn was made in exchange for a debt (consideration). However, if the pawned item (a slave) has value exceeding the debt, and the slave dies in the pledgee's hands, the witnesses become liable for the excess value because their testimony caused the pledgee to be in possession. If the pawner claims pawn and the pledgee denies, the witnesses are not liable for the excess (since they testified for the pawner against the pledgee?), but they become liable for the amount of the debt to the pledgee. If the witnesses retract about the pawn but not about delivery, saying 'he delivered the slave but not as pawn', they are not liable.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): Pawn; a contract where property is given as security for a debt.
الفضل (al-fadl): Excess; the value of the pawned property above the amount of debt.
الأصل (al-asl): The original case; the pawn transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two witnesses testify that a slave worth 200 was pawned for a debt of 100. The slave dies in the pledgee's hands, then the witnesses retract. What are they liable for?
A) The full value of the slave (200)
B) They are not liable at all
C) The excess of 100 over the debt
D) Only the debt amount of 100

Key Takeaway
Witnesses who retract testimony about a pawn are only liable for the excess value if the pawned property perishes; otherwise, they bear no liability.
