Liability for Mahr When Two Pairs of Witnesses Retract
Ruling on the apportionment of damages when witnesses of divorce and consummation retract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the liability (ḍamān) of retracting witnesses in the case of two pairs for divorce and consummation, applying the principle that liability corresponds to what was established by each pair’s testimony.

Core Idea
Liability for retraction is apportioned according to what each witness pair's testimony established and caused to be lost.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq explains a nuanced ruling on witness liability (ḍamān) when two pairs of witnesses retract their testimony in a marriage case: one pair testified to divorce, the other to consummation. Understanding the apportionment of liability is essential for applying the Hanafi rule that each retracting party is liable only for the portion of the mahr that was proven exclusively by their testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two witness pairs and their testimonies
The case involves two pairs of witnesses: one pair testified to divorce, the other to consummation. The testimony of the divorce witnesses established only half the mahr (since divorce before consummation reduces the mahr to half). The consummation witnesses established the full mahr.
Under Hanafi law, if a husband divorces before consummation, the wife is entitled to half the specified mahr.
2
Determine the effect of each pair's retraction
When the divorce witnesses retract, no liability falls on them because the consummation witnesses still establish the full mahr, so no loss is caused by their retraction. When the consummation witnesses retract, they are liable for half the mahr because their testimony had proven the full mahr, and their retraction causes the loss of the half that was exclusively proven by them.
The divorce witnesses’ testimony only proved half, so their retraction does not diminish the total proven mahr.
3
Calculate liability when one from each pair retracts
If one divorce witness and one consummation witness retract, the divorce witnesses owe nothing because the remaining divorce witness still proves half the mahr, and the consummation witness’s retraction causes loss of half? Actually, the text says: nothing on the divorce witnesses, and on the consummation witnesses a quarter. Let’s parse: The consummation witnesses originally proved the full mahr. After one retracts, the remaining consummation witness still proves half the mahr (since two witnesses are needed for full proof?). Under Hanafi law, one witness alone is not sufficient for full proof; two witnesses are needed. So retraction of one consummation witness reduces the proof to half. Thus, the loss caused by that retraction is half of what was proven by the pair? The text says the liability is a quarter of the total mahr. Let’s verify: The divorce witnesses prove half the mahr. If both divorce witnesses retract, no loss because consummation witnesses still prove full. If one divorce witness retracts, still half is proven by the remaining divorce witness, so no loss from divorce side. As for consummation: originally they proved full mahr (100 mithqal). After one retracts, only half the mahr (50) can be proven by the remaining consummation witness? But the divorce witnesses already prove half (50)? Actually the scenario: total mahr 100. Divorce testimony proves 50 (half). Consummation testimony proves 100 (full). After one consummation witness retracts, what is proven? The remaining consummation witness alone proves nothing? Actually, in Hanafi law, one witness is not enough; so the testimony of the one remaining consummation witness is ineffective, leaving only the divorce testimony which proves 50. Thus the loss caused by retraction of one consummation witness is the difference between what was originally proven (100) and what remains (50), i.e. 50. But the text says liability is a quarter (25). Wait, the text says: 'If from each group one retracts, nothing is obligatory upon the witnesses of divorce, and upon the witnesses of consummation is obligatory the quarter.' That suggests the liability is 1/4 of the total mahr. Let's re-evaluate: The passage likely means that when one from each pair retracts, the remaining divorce witness still proves half the mahr (50), and the remaining consummation witness also proves half? Actually, one witness from consummation can prove half? No, a single witness cannot prove any amount; but maybe the context is that the testimony of the consummation witnesses itself proved the full mahr, but when one retracts, the other’s testimony alone proves half? Under Hanafi law, one witness can prove half of the claim if coupled with an oath? Not here. The text does not provide the detailed reasoning; it states the result. For the purpose of this lesson, we will simply note the outcome: no liability on divorce witnesses, quarter on consummation witnesses, and the student should accept it as given. The guided path should focus on the first two cases where reasoning is clearer.
The text gives the result without full reasoning; students should memorize the three outcomes.
4
Apply the principle to other scenarios
The underlying principle is that liability for retraction equals the portion of the mahr that was proven exclusively by the retracting pair and would have been lost due to their retraction.
This is consistent with the Hanafi rule that the retracting witness is liable for the amount that would have been payable based on his testimony alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورجلان بالدخول ثم رجع شاهدا الطلاق لا ضمان عليهما لأنهما أوجبا نصف المهر وشاهدا الدخول أوجبا جميع المهر وقد بقي من يثبت بشهادته جميع المهر وهو شاهدا الدخول، وإن شاهد الدخول لا غير يجب عليهما نصف المهر لأنه يثبت بشهادة شهود الطلاق نصف المهر وتلف بشهادة شاهدي الدخول نصف المهر، وإن رجع من كل طائفة واحد لا يجب على شاهدي الطلاق شيء ويجب على شاهدي الدخول الربع اهـ.
And [if there are] two men [witnesses] for consummation, then the witnesses of divorce retract: no liability upon them because they obligated half the dower, and the witnesses of consummation obligated the entire dower; and there remains someone by whose testimony the entire dower is established, and they are the witnesses of consummation. And if the witnesses of consummation retract — none other — then upon them is half the dower, because by the testimony of the witnesses of divorce half the dower is established, and by the retraction of the witnesses of consummation half the dower is lost. And if from each group one retracts, nothing is obligatory upon the witnesses of divorce, and upon the witnesses of consummation is obligatory the quarter. End.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the liability (damān) of witnesses when they retract their testimony in a case involving two pairs: one pair testified to divorce, the other to consummation. The ruling specifies the amounts of mahr (dower) that become due upon retraction. The key principle is that liability corresponds to what was established by each pair's testimony. The witnesses of divorce only established half the mahr (since divorce before consummation reduces the mahr to half), so their retraction causes no loss because the consummation witnesses still establish the full mahr. Conversely, the consummation witnesses established the full mahr, so their retraction leads to liability for half the mahr (the part that was exclusively proven by them). When one from each pair retracts, the remaining witnesses still provide partial proof, so the liability is reduced proportionally. The word 'اهـ' indicates the end of a quoted passage.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for financial loss caused by one's action, here retraction of testimony.
شاهدا الدخول (shāhidā al-dukhūl): The two witnesses who testified to the consummation of marriage.
شاهدا الطلاق (shāhidā al-ṭalāq): The two witnesses who testified to the divorce.
المهر (al-mahr): Dower or bridal gift owed by husband to wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The two witnesses of consummation
C) All four equally
D) None of them

Key Takeaway
Liability for retraction of testimony is determined by what portion of the claim was exclusively proven by the retracting witnesses and lost due to their retraction.

Liability of Retracting Witnesses in Conflicting Divorce Testimony
Ruling on retraction with conflicting testimony and unnamed mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for determining liability of retracting witnesses when testimony conflicts on the number of divorces, and identify when the liability shifts from mahr to mut'ah.

Core Idea
Only testimony that influences the judge's ruling creates liability upon retraction; ineffective testimony does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the consequences of retraction when two sets of witnesses give conflicting accounts of a divorce. The key is that only the testimony accepted by the judge can cause a loss, and thus only those witnesses are liable for damages. The ruling also covers cases where no specific mahr was named, shifting liability to the mut'ah gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting testimonies
Two witnesses claim triple divorce, two claim single divorce. Determine which testimony the judge would accept based on the stronger effect.
Triple divorce creates severe prohibition (ḥurma ghalīẓa), single creates mild prohibition (ḥurma khafīfa).
2
Determine which testimony was effective
The judge accepts the triple divorce testimony because it has a greater legal effect. The single divorce testimony is not accepted as it would be redundant.
If both were accepted, the ruling would be contradictory.
3
Apply liability rule upon retraction
Only the witnesses whose testimony was accepted (triple) are liable for half the mahr because their retraction causes loss of that half. The single witnesses are not liable.
Liability requires that the testimony caused a compensable loss.
4
Distinguish after consummation
If divorce occurs after consummation, the number of divorces does not affect the mahr obligation (full mahr is due regardless), so no loss results from retraction. No liability.
After consummation, divorce is effective irrespective of the number testified.
5
Handle unnamed mahr cases
If no mahr was specified, the obligatory gift is mut'ah. Retracting witnesses who caused loss of mut'ah must compensate for the mut'ah, not a named mahr. Example from al-Muheet illustrates settlement scenarios.
The judge's award determines what is lost; if the judge awarded a slave, the witnesses guarantee the slave's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : شهدا أنه طلق امرأته ثلاثاً وآخران أنه طلقها واحدة قبل الدخول ثم رجعوا فضمان نصف المهر على شهود الثلاث لا غير لأنه لم يقض بشهادة شهود الواحد لأنه لا يفيد لأن حكم الواحدة حرمة خفيفة وحكم الثلاث حرمة غليظة، ولو كان بعد الدخول فلا ضمان على أحد اهـ. وأشار بالمهر إلى أن الكلام فيما إذا كان مسمى، فلو لم يكن مسمى ضمنا المتعة لأنها الواجبة وقد أتلفاها. وفي المحيط : تزوجها بلا مهر وطلقها قبل الدخول فشهدا أنه صالحها من المتعة على عبد وقبضته وهي تنكر ثم رجعا لا يضمنان العبد بل المتعة وإن كان مهر مثلها عشرة ضمنا لها خمسة دراهم لأن القاضي لم يقض لها بالعبد لكونه مقبوضاً فقد أتلفا بشهادتهما على المرأة المتعة لا العبد بخلاف ما لو شهدا أنه صالحها عنها بعبد وقضى لها به ثم شهدا بقبضه ثم رجعا ضمنا قيمة العبد لوقوع القضاء بالعبد .اهـ.
Then he said: Two witnesses testified that he divorced his wife three times, and two others testified that he divorced her once before consummation, then they retracted. So the liability for half the dower is on the witnesses of three only, because the testimony of the witnesses of one was not accepted as it does not benefit; for the ruling of one divorce is a mild prohibition, and the ruling of three is a severe prohibition. And if it were after consummation, there is no liability upon anyone. End. And he pointed with 'mahr' to the fact that the discussion is when it is named; if it were not named, they owe the mut'ah (consolatory gift) because it is obligatory and they caused its loss. And in al-Muheet: He married her without a mahr and divorced her before consummation, and two witnesses testified that he settled with her for the mut'ah for a slave and she took possession of it, while she denies. Then they retract: they do not guarantee the slave but rather the mut'ah. And if her mahr equivalent was ten, they owe her five dirhams because the judge did not award her the slave since it was in her possession; so they caused loss to the woman through their testimony regarding the mut'ah, not the slave. Unlike if they testified that he settled with her for a slave and the judge awarded it to her, then they testified to her possession, then retracted: they guarantee the value of the slave because the judgment for the slave occurred. End.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage expands on rulings for retraction when two pairs of witnesses give different accounts of the number of divorces. The first scenario involves divorce before consummation: witnesses for triple divorce (thalāth) and witnesses for single divorce (wāḥida). The judge accepts the testimony that gives the stronger effect (triple), so only the triple witnesses are considered to have caused loss when they retract; the single witnesses' testimony was ineffective from the start, so no liability. After consummation, divorce is effective regardless of number, so no loss occurs from retraction. Then the text discusses cases where no specific mahr was named (ghayr musammā). In that case, the obligatory gift is mut'ah, so retracting witnesses guarantee the mut'ah, not a named mahr. The 'Muheet' example illustrates a settlement where the witnesses falsely claimed a settlement with a slave; since the slave was not awarded by the judge (because possession was already claimed by the woman), the loss is the mut'ah, not the slave. However, if the judge had awarded the slave based on their testimony, they would guarantee its value.

Key Terms
حرمة خفيفة (ḥurma khafīfa): Mild prohibition: a single divorce does not make the woman completely forbidden; reconciliation is possible.
حرمة غليظة (ḥurma ghalīẓa): Severe prohibition: three divorces make the woman completely forbidden until she marries another man and is divorced.
متعة (mut'ah): A consolatory gift given to the wife upon divorce if no mahr was specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both pairs equally
B) The witnesses of triple divorce only
C) The witnesses of single divorce only
D) None

Key Takeaway
Witnesses are liable only for the loss caused by testimony that was judicially effective; ineffective testimony creates no liability.

Retraction of Divorce Witnesses After Husband's Death: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on retraction of divorce witnesses after husband's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of retracting divorce witnesses after the husband's death and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the inward effect of a judge's decree based on false testimony.

Core Idea
Whether a judge's decree changes inward reality determines inheritance rights.

This lesson examines a scenario from al-Bahr al-Raiq where witnesses of a pre-consumption divorce retract after the husband's death. The central question is: do the witnesses owe the heirs half the mahr, and does the wife inherit? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn disagree based on a deeper usul principle: the extent to which a judge's decree (especially one based on false testimony) affects the inward reality of a marriage. Understanding this dispute sharpens our grasp of the interplay between judicial authority and factual reality in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case involves witnesses who testified to a divorce before consummation. The husband dies, and the witnesses later retract. The ruling concerns what the witnesses owe and whether the wife inherits.
The retraction occurs after the husband's death, making the heirs the claimants.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that the witnesses owe the heirs half the mahr and the wife does not inherit. This is because the judge's decree of divorce is effective both outwardly and inwardly (ẓāhiran wa bāṭinan), so the marriage is fully dissolved.
The witnesses stand in place of the deceased (as heirs) for liability.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the wife inherits and the witnesses do not owe the inheritance. They argue the decree is only outwardly effective; inwardly the marriage remains valid.
The witnesses are not liable for the inheritance because the loss arises from the decree, not from their testimony alone.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The disagreement turns on the principle of nufādh al-qaḍā': does a judge's decree based on false testimony change the inward reality? Abu Hanifa says yes; the Sahibayn say no, distinguishing between external rulings (like dower) and internal facts (like marital status).
This is a key usul al-fiqh point with far-reaching implications.
5
Note the condition of liability and exception
The passage notes that after consummation, witnesses are not liable because there is no similarity (mumāthala) between the marital bond (bud‘) and property. This condition restricts the general rule to cases before consummation.
The text from al-Muheet also presents a variant involving two sets of witnesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال قبل الوطء والخلوة» لكان أولى وإن كانت كالوطء في إيجاب المهر. وأطلق في ضمانها فشمل ما بعد موت الزوج لما في المحيط : شهود الطلاق قبل الدخول إذا رجعوا بعد موت الزوج ضمنا لورثته نصف المهر لأنهم قائمون مقام المورث ولا ميراث للمرأة ادعت الطلاق أو لا أقرت الورثة أنه طلقها أو لا وهذا قول أبي حنيفة. وقالا : ترث ولا يضمن الشاهدان ميراثها بناء على أن قضاء القاضي بالطلاق بشهادة الزور ينفذ ظاهراً وباطناً عنده خلافاً لهما، ولو شهدا بذلك بعد موت الزوج وادعى ذلك الورثة فقضى لها بنصف المهر ثم رجعا ضمنا للمرأة نصف المهر والميراث اهـ . قوله : (ولم يضمنا لو بعد (الوطء لأن من شرط الضمان المماثلة ولا مماثلة بين البضع والمال وقد ذكره الأصوليون في بحث القضاء. وفي المحيط : شهدا على الطلاق وآخران على الدخول ولم يفرض لها مهر ثم رجعوا ضمن شاهدا الطلاق نصف المتعة وشاهدا الدخول بقية المهر اهـ.
And if he said 'before intercourse and seclusion' it would be more appropriate, even though [seclusion] is like intercourse in obligating the dower. And [the author] made it absolute regarding their liability, so it includes after the death of the husband, as in al-Muheet: Witnesses of divorce before consummation, if they retract after the husband's death, they owe his heirs half the dower because they stand in the place of the deceased, and the woman has no inheritance whether she claimed divorce or not, whether the heirs admitted that he divorced her or not. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: She inherits, and the two witnesses do not guarantee her inheritance, based on the fact that the judge's decree of divorce based on false testimony is effective externally and internally according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them. And if they testified to that after the husband's death and the heirs claimed it, and he decreed for her half the dower, then they retract, they owe the woman half the dower and the inheritance. End. His statement: 'And they are not liable if after intercourse' because one of the conditions of liability is similarity, and there is no similarity between the vulva and property, as mentioned by the scholars of usul in the discussion of adjudication. And in al-Muheet: Two testified to divorce and two others to consummation, and no dower was fixed for her, then they retracted: the witnesses of divorce owe half the mut'ah, and the witnesses of consummation owe the remainder of the dower. End.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage addresses the retraction of divorce witnesses after the husband's death. The main rule: witnesses owe the heirs half the mahr because they caused the loss to the estate. The woman does not inherit because the divorce is proven (even if by false testimony) and a divorced woman does not inherit. Abu Hanifa holds that the decree of divorce is effective both externally and inwardly (ẓāhiran wa bāṭinan), so even if the testimony was false, the marriage is dissolved completely. Thus, the woman has no inheritance rights. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the decree is only effective externally, not inwardly, so the marriage remains valid inwardly, and thus the woman inherits. In that case, the witnesses are not liable for the inheritance because the loss is due to the decree, not their testimony. However, if the retraction occurs after the woman has already received half the mahr by decree, then the witnesses must compensate her for both the mahr and the inheritance. The text then notes a point: after consummation, witnesses are not liable because the loss (the marriage bond) is not compensable as property (no similarity between 'bud' and property). Finally, a scenario from al-Muheet with no fixed mahr: witnesses of divorce owe half mut'ah, witnesses of consummation owe the remaining mahr.

Key Terms
القضاء بالطلاق بشهادة الزور (al-qaḍā' bi-l-ṭalāq bi-shahādat al-zūr): A judge's decree of divorce based on false testimony.
نفاذ القضاء ظاهراً وباطناً (nufādh al-qaḍā' ẓāhiran wa bāṭinan): The decree is effective both in external rulings (like dower) and internal reality (like inheritance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does the wife inherit from her husband when the divorce witnesses retract after his death?
A) Because the judge's decree is effective both outwardly and inwardly, so the marriage is dissolved.
B) Because the judge's decree is only effective outwardly, so the marriage remains valid inwardly.
C) Because the witnesses owe the heirs the dower, which compensates the wife.
D) Because the wife's claim of divorce is automatically rejected after the husband's death.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a judge's decree on inward reality is a contested usul point that directly impacts inheritance rights in cases of false testimony.

Liability of Retracting Witnesses in Khul' and Nafaqah
Ruling on witness liability for retraction in khul' and nafaqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of retracting witnesses in khul' and nafaqah cases and distinguish between the two scenarios.

Core Idea
Retracting witnesses cause financial loss; they owe the amount they falsely deprived the claimant.

When witnesses testify to a fact that causes a woman to lose her right to dower or maintenance, then retract, they become liable for the loss. This rule applies differently whether the khul' occurs before or after consummation, and whether the nafaqah was a debt. Understanding these distinctions ensures correct application of liability law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two separate cases: khul' testimony and nafaqah testimony. Identify the key difference: in khul', the wife is alleged to have released the husband from dower; in nafaqah, she is alleged to have received maintenance.
2
Analyze the khul' case
Determine the dower status: before consummation, only half the dower is due; after consummation, the full dower is due. Witnesses who falsely claim she released the husband cause loss accordingly.
The witnesses 'obligated that upon her without compensation' – they caused a liability.
3
Analyze the nafaqah case
The judge decreed nafaqah and witnesses claimed she received it. Upon retraction, they owe the amount because she lost her right to collect. For relatives' nafaqah, assess whether it became a debt (e.g., via a borrowing order).
If the nafaqah did not become a debt, some scholars say no liability.
4
Apply the 'loss caused' principle
In both cases, the witnesses' false testimony caused the judgment to be based on falsehood, resulting in the woman losing her claim. Liability is based on the amount of financial loss they caused.
This is a form of damān (civil liability).
5
Resolve the intra-Hanafi difference
For nafaqah of relatives, some held that retracting witnesses are not liable because the nafaqah is not a debt. The preferred view, adopted in the text, treats it as a debt if the judge ordered borrowing, thus witnesses are liable.
This shows how jurists interpret the underlying legal cause (ʿilla).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يناسب هذا النوع مسألتا الشهادة بالخلع والنفقة ; أما الأول ففي المحيط : شهدا على امرأة أنها اختلعت من زوجها قبل الدخول على أنها أبرأته من المهر وهي تجحد ضمنا لها نصف المهر لأنهما أوجبا عليها ذلك بغير عوض، ولو كان دخل بها يضمنان كل المهر اهـ. وأما النفقة ففي المحيط فرض القاضي لها النفقة أو المتعة ثم شهدا بالاستيفاء وقضى ثم رجعا ضمنا للمرأة وكذلك نفقة الأقارب قيل في نفقة الأقارب سهو لأنها لا تصير ديناً بقضاء فما أتلفا شيئاً، وقيل إنها مؤولة وتأويلها أن القاضي قضى له وأمره بالاستدانة عليه حتى يرجع بما استدان على المقضى عليه بالنفقة وقد استدان وصار ديناً له على المقضى عليه فقد شهدا عليه باستيفاء دين مستحق له على المقضى عليه فضمنا بالرجوع اهـ.
And among what is suitable for this type are the two issues of testimony regarding khul' and nafaqah. As for the first, in al-Muheet: Two witnesses testified against a woman that she divorced herself (khul') from her husband before consummation on condition that she release him from the dower, while she denies it: they owe her half the dower because they obligated that upon her without compensation. And if he had consummated with her, they guarantee the entire dower. End. As for nafaqah, in al-Muheet: The judge prescribed for her nafaqah or mut'ah, then they testified to receipt, and he decreed, then they retracted: they owe the woman. And likewise the nafaqah of relatives. It was said that regarding nafaqah of relatives it is a mistake, because it does not become a debt by decree, so they did not cause any loss. And it was said that it is interpreted, and its interpretation is that the judge decreed for him and ordered him to borrow against it until he recovers what he borrowed from the person obligated for nafaqah, and he borrowed and it became a debt for him against the obligated person, so they testified against him for the receipt of a debt due to him from the obligated person, so they are liable upon retraction. End.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents two additional scenarios related to retraction of testimony: khul' (divorce by redemption) and nafaqah (maintenance). In khul', if witnesses falsely claim a woman made khul' before consummation, releasing her husband from the dower, they owe her half the dower because they caused her to lose that right without any return. After consummation, the entire mahr was due, so they owe the full amount. For nafaqah, if the judge had decreed nafaqah or mut'ah for her, and witnesses then testify that she received it, and later retract, they owe the woman the amount because they caused the loss of her right to receive payment through their false testimony. There is a discussion about nafaqah for relatives (e.g., children or parents): some say it is a mistake to hold witnesses liable because such nafaqah is not a debt until decreed; but the preferred view is that if the judge ordered the obligated person to borrow and pay, then the recipient has a debt, so retracting witnesses cause loss and are liable.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation to the husband; here, the wife releases the husband from the mahr.
نفقة الأقارب (nafaqat al-aqārib): Maintenance obligated to close relatives like parents and children.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; monetary compensation for loss caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a nafaqah case for relatives, what must have occurred for the retracting witnesses to be liable according to the preferred view?
A) The judge must have decreed nafaqah
B) The judge must have ordered the obligated person to borrow and pay
C) The relatives must be poor
D) The witnesses must have testified falsely

Key Takeaway
Liability of retracting witnesses depends on the existence of a financial loss—the amount they falsely deprived the claimant of, which varies with the type of case and conditions like consummation or borrowing order.

Retraction in Testimony of Emancipation: Liability and Value
Ruling on retraction in emancipation testimony and value liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of retracting witnesses in emancipation cases and identify the effect of subsequent confession according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Witnesses who falsely testify to emancipation owe the slave's value at judgment; patronage remains with the master.

This lesson delves into the complex fiqh of retraction in testimony for emancipation. It covers the core principle that retracting witnesses are liable for the value of the slave, and then examines various scenarios including mixed testimonies, timing of valuation, and the effect of a subsequent confession. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying the rules of shahada in financial and personal liberty cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its rationale
Understand that retracting witnesses in emancipation cases are liable for the value of the slave because they caused the master to lose his property without compensation. The patronage (walā') remains with the original manumitter, not the witnesses.
The rationale is that the witnesses' testimony led to the loss of the slave's value, and the compensation is for that loss.
2
Analyze the scenario of emancipation with a price
If the slave was emancipated for a price (e.g., 500 when the value is 1000), the master may claim the full value from the witnesses, and the witnesses can then recover the price from the slave.
This shows that the liability is for the full value, not just the price paid.
3
Examine mixed testimonies (emancipation + adultery) and liability allocation
When testimonies for emancipation and adultery lead to execution, the witnesses of emancipation owe the slave's value to the master, and the witnesses of adultery owe blood money. The master cannot take both if he denies emancipation, but the decree overrides his denial.
The value and blood money are separate compensations for different losses (monetary value and life).
4
Determine the time of valuation and its implications
The value is assessed on the day the judge decrees emancipation, because the actual loss (deprivation of the slave) occurs at that point. Between the alleged emancipation and the judgment, the slave is treated as free regarding his actions (e.g., punishments, injury compensation).
This prevents witnesses from being liable for a different value than the one at the time of loss.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of a subsequent confession
If the master/husband confesses to emancipation/divorce after the decree, Abu Hanifa holds that the decree is inwardly effective, so the confession is invalid and witnesses remain liable. Sahibayn hold that the decree is only outwardly effective, so the confession is valid and witnesses are discharged.
This dispute affects whether the witnesses can get a refund of any damages they paid.
6
Apply the rules to the case of tadbīr vs immediate emancipation
When witnesses for tadbīr (emancipation upon death) and others for immediate emancipation retract, the tadbīr witnesses are liable for the decrease in value caused by tadbīr, and the emancipation witnesses are liable for the full value as a mudabbar.
Because the tadbīr decree was valid at the time, and the emancipation witnesses caused the loss of the slave from ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي العتق ضمنا القيمة لأنهما أتلفا مالية العبد عليه من غير عوض والولاء للمعتق لأن العتق لا يتحول إليهما بهذا الضمان وهو لا يصلح عوضاً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانا أو ضمان إتلاف الملك بخلاف ضمان الإعتاق لأنه لم يتلف إلا ملكه ولزم منه فساد ملك صاحبه فضمنه الشارع صلة ومواساة له أطلق العتق فانصرف إلى العتق بلا مال فلو شهدا أنه أعتق عبده على خمسمائة وقيمته ألف فقضى ثم رجعا إن شاء ضمن الشاهدين الألف ورجعا على العبد بخمسمائة وولاء العبد للمولى؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي البزازية : شهدا على رجل بإعتاق عبده وأربعة أخر أنه زنى وهو محصن فحكم بالعتق والرجم ورجم ثم رجعوا فالقيمة على شهود العتق للمولى والدية على شهود الزنا للمولى أيضاً إن لم يكن له وراث آخر، والمولى إن كان جاحداً للعتق يمنع أخذ الدية لكن زعمه باطل بالحكم وصار كالمعدوم، ووجوب القيمة بدل المالية ووجوب الدية بدل النفس، ثم الدية للمقتول حتى تقضى بها ديونه فلا يلزم بدلان عن مبدل واحد اهـ. ولو شهدا أنه أعتق عبده عام الأول في رمضان وقضى القاضي بعتقه ثم رجعا ضمنا قيمة العبد يوم أعتقه القاضي وحكمه في حدوده وجزاء جناية فيما بين رمضان إلى أن أعتقه القاضي حكم الحر لأن القاضي أثبت حريته من رمضان بالبينة، والثابتة بالبينة العادلة كالثابت بالمعاينة، وفي حق إيجاب الضمان يعتبر حراً يوم القضاء لأن التلف حصل يوم القضاء لأن المنع والحيلولة بين المولى وعبده حصل يوم القضاء. ولو شهدا أنه طلق امرأته عام أوّل في رمضان قبل الدخول وقضى به وألزمه نصف المهر ثم رجعا وضمنا ثم شهد آخران أنه طلقها عام أول في شوّال قبل الدخول بها لم تقبل ولا يقع الأولان لأنها صارت مبانة بالطلاق الأول قبل الدخول فلا يتصوّر تطليقها بعد ذلك فكانت الشهادة الأخيرة باطلة وبقي الضمان على الفريق الأول بحاله، ولو أقر الزوج بذلك يرد على الشاهدين ما ضمنا، وكذلك إقرار المولى بالعتق قيل هذا عند أبي يوسف ومحمد خلافاً لأبي حنيفة بناء على نفاذ القضاء باطناً فمتى نفذ القضاء في رمضان باطناً عنده لم يصح إقراره بالطلاق والعتاق في شوّال من هذا العام فبقي التلف مضافاً إلى شهادتهما لا إلى إقراره وعندهما لما لم ينفذ القضاء باطناً بقي النكاح والرق إلى شوّال باطناً فصح إقراره في شوّال وكان التلف مضافاً إلى إقراره لا إلى الشهادة؛ كذا في المحيط ثم قال : ولو شهدا بالتدبير وآخران بالعتق فرجعوا فالضمان على شهود العتق لأن القضاء بالتدبير مع العتق لا يفيد لأن حكم التدبير بقاء الرق إلى وقت الموت، ولا يبقى الرق مع العتق البات فلا يقضى بالتدبير، فإن قضى بشهادة التدبير ثم شهد آخران بالعتق البات فقضى به ثم رجعوا ضمن شهود التدبير ما نقصه التدبير وشهود العتق قيمته مدبراً لأن القضاء بالتدبير يفيد حكمه لأنه ليس حالة القضاء بالتدبير شهادة قائمة بالعتق فأمكن القضاء بالتدبير، وشاهد العتق أزالا المدبر عن ملكه بغير عوض فيضمنان قيمته مدبراً اهـ.
His statement: 'And in emancipation, they are liable for the value because they caused the loss of the slave's monetary value from him without compensation, and the patronage (walā') is for the manumitter, because emancipation does not transfer to them through this liability, and it is not suitable as compensation.' He made it absolute so it includes whether they... or liability for destruction of property, unlike liability for emancipation, because he only destroyed his ownership and it necessitated the corruption of his companion's ownership, so the Lawgiver made him liable as a gift and consolation for him. He made emancipation absolute, so it applies to emancipation without money. So if they testified that he emancipated his slave for five hundred and its value is a thousand, and he decreed, then they retract: if he wishes, he may hold the two witnesses liable for the thousand, and they have recourse against the slave for five hundred, and the patronage of the slave is for the master. Thus in al-Muheet. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Two testified against a man for the emancipation of his slave, and four others that he committed adultery while married, so he (the judge) decreed emancipation and stoning, and he was stoned, then they retracted: the value is upon the witnesses of emancipation for the master, and the blood money is upon the witnesses of adultery for the master as well if he has no other heir; and if the master denies the emancipation, he is prevented from taking the blood money, but his claim is nullified by the decree and he becomes like a non-entity. And the obligation of value is in place of the monetary value, and the obligation of blood money is in place of the life; then the blood money is for the slain person until his debts are paid, so there are not two compensations for one object. End. And if they testified that he emancipated his slave in the first year in Ramadan, and the judge decreed his emancipation, then they retract: they owe the value of the slave on the day the judge emancipated him; and his ruling regarding his legal punishments and compensation for injury between Ramadan and the time the judge emancipated him is the ruling of a free person, because the judge established his freedom from Ramadan by evidence; that which is established by upright evidence is like that established by eyewitness. And regarding the obligation of liability, he is considered free on the day of the judgment because the loss occurred on the day of judgment, because the prevention and obstruction between the master and his slave occurred on the day of judgment. And if they testified that he divorced his wife in the first year in Ramadan before consummation, and he decreed it and imposed half the dower, then they retracted and were liable, then two others testified that he divorced her in the first year in Shawwal before consummation: it is not accepted, and the first (divorce) does not occur, because she became separated by the first divorce before consummation, so it is impossible to divorce her after that; so the latter testimony is invalid, and the liability on the first group remains as it was. And if the husband confesses to that, what they were liable for is repaid to the witnesses, and likewise the confession of the master to emancipation. It is said this is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Hanifa, based on the inward enforceability of the decree. So when the decree is inwardly enforceable in Ramadan according to him, his confession to divorce and emancipation in Shawwal of this year is not valid, so the loss remains attributed to their testimony, not to his confession. According to them, since the decree is not inwardly enforceable, the marriage and slavery remain inwardly until Shawwal, so his confession in Shawwal is valid, and the loss is attributed to his confession, not to the testimony. Thus in al-Muheet. Then he said: And if two testified for tadbīr (slavery with emancipation upon death) and two others for immediate emancipation, and they retract: the liability is on the witnesses of emancipation, because the decree for tadbīr together with emancipation does not benefit, for the ruling of tadbīr is the continuation of slavery until death, and slavery does not continue with absolute emancipation; so the judge does not decree tadbīr. So if he decreed based on the testimony of tadbīr, then two others testified for absolute emancipation and he decreed based on it, then they retract: the witnesses of tadbīr are liable for the decrease caused by tadbīr, and the witnesses of emancipation are liable for its value as a mudabbar (slave with tadbīr), because the decree for tadbīr benefits its effect, since at the time of the decree for tadbīr there was no standing testimony for emancipation; so it was possible to decree tadbīr. And the witnesses of emancipation removed the mudabbar from his (the master's) ownership without compensation, so they guarantee its value as a mudabbar. End.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers retraction of testimony in cases of emancipation ('itq). The key rule: witnesses who falsely testify to a slave's emancipation are liable for the slave's value (qīmah) because they caused the master to lose his property without any return. The patronage (walā') remains with the original manumitter (the master), not with the witnesses, because the emancipation itself does not transfer to them through compensation. The text then gives examples: if the emancipation was for a price (e.g., 500, when the slave is worth 1000), the master may choose to claim the full value from the witnesses, and they can recover the price from the slave. Next, a case from al-Bazzaziyya: witnesses testify to emancipation and others to adultery, leading to the slave being freed and stoned. Upon retraction, the emancipation witnesses owe the value, and the adultery witnesses owe blood money to the master (if no other heir). The master cannot take both if he denies emancipation, but the decree overrides his denial. Then a scenario about time: if witnesses testify to emancipation in Ramadan, and the judge decrees accordingly, and they retract later, the value is assessed at the time of the judgment because the actual loss (deprivation of the slave) occurs at that point. Between Ramadan and the judgment, the slave is treated as free regarding his actions (e.g., punishments, injury compensation). Another scenario: witnesses for divorce in Ramadan, then later others for divorce in Shawwal; the latter is invalid because the woman is already divorced. If the husband later confesses to divorce, the witnesses' liability may be refunded depending on the inward effect of the decree—an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds the decree is inwardly effective so the confession is void and witnesses remain liable; the Sahibayn say the confession is valid and the witnesses are discharged. Finally, the case of tadbīr (slavery with emancipation upon death) vs absolute emancipation: if witnesses for tadbīr testify first and the judge decrees tadbīr, then later witnesses for absolute emancipation testify and the judge decrees that, and then all retract, the tadbīr witnesses are liable for the decrease in value caused by tadbīr, and the emancipation witnesses are liable for the full value as a mudabbar (because they caused the loss of the slave from the master's ownership). The reasoning is that the preliminary tadbīr decree was valid at the time, and the later emancipation witnesses removed the slave from ownership.

Key Terms
ولاء (walā'): Patronage: the relationship between the master and the freed slave, including inheritance rights.
تدبير (tadbīr): A form of emancipation where the master declares that the slave will be free upon the master's death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been given tadbīr.
قيمة (qīmah): Market value; the compensation that retracting witnesses must pay for the loss of the slave.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession; a declaration by the master that he did emancipate or divorce, which can affect liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the liability of retracting witnesses in an emancipation case if the emancipation was alleged to have occurred with a price?
A) They pay the price only.
B) They pay the full value and can recover the price from the slave.
C) They pay nothing because the price covers the loss.
D) They pay half the value.

Key Takeaway
Retracting witnesses in emancipation cases are liable for the slave's value at the time of judgment, and the patronage remains with the master; a subsequent confession may affect liability depending on the inward effect of the decree.

Witnesses Retracting Manumission Testimony: Liability and Wala
States ruling for witnesses retracting manumission testimony and subcase with price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses who retract manumission testimony, explain the basis of liability (itlaf), and apply the ruling to the subcase where the manumission was for a price.

Core Idea
Witnesses who retract manumission testimony guarantee full value, but wala remains with the manumittor—compensation does not transfer patronage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding witnesses who retract their testimony after a judge has ruled on manumission. It addresses both simple manumission and manumission for a price, clarifying that the witnesses' liability is for destroying the owner's property, and that wala (patronage) cannot be transferred through financial compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The retraction of testimony in a manumission case. The ruling: witnesses must pay the full value of the slave.
The ruling applies to cases where retraction occurs after the judge has ruled based on that testimony.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
Why are they liable? Because they destroyed the owner's property value without compensation. The Lawgiver imposes liability as a gift and consolation to the owner.
The liability is for property destruction (itlaf al-milk), not for manumission itself.
3
Distinguish the subcase with a price
If they testified that manumission was for a price (e.g., 500 dirhams for a slave valued at 1000), the owner may claim the full 1000 from the witnesses, who then recover 500 from the freed slave.
The witnesses have recourse against the slave for the price paid.
4
Apply the wala rule
In all cases, wala (patronage rights) remains with the original manumittor. It does not transfer to the witnesses, even if they pay compensation.
Wala is a personal relationship based on manumission, not a transferable property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي العتق ضمنا القيمة لأنهما أتلفا مالية العبد عليه من غير عوض والولاء للمعتق لأن العتق لا يتحول إليهما بهذا الضمان وهو لا يصلح عوضاً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانا أو ضمان إتلاف الملك بخلاف ضمان الإعتاق لأنه لم يتلف إلا ملكه ولزم منه فساد ملك صاحبه فضمنه الشارع صلة ومواساة له أطلق العتق فانصرف إلى العتق بلا مال فلو شهدا أنه أعتق عبده على خمسمائة وقيمته ألف فقضى ثم رجعا إن شاء ضمن الشاهدين الألف ورجعا على العبد بخمسمائة وولاء العبد للمولى؛ كذا في المحيط.
His saying: (And in manumission, they guarantee the value because they destroyed the slave's property value upon him without compensation, and the wala is for the manumittor because manumission does not transfer to them through this guarantee and it is not suitable as compensation. He [the author] made it absolute so it includes both [the case of] or guarantee of destruction of property in contrast to guarantee of manumission because he only destroyed his own property and it necessitated the corruption of his partner's property, so the Lawgiver made him liable as a gift and consolation to him. He made manumission absolute so it refers to manumission without money. So if they testified that he manumitted his slave for five hundred and its value is one thousand, and the judge ruled, then they retracted, he may, if he wishes, hold the two witnesses liable for the thousand, and they recover from the slave five hundred, and the wala of the slave belongs to the master; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling from the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) regarding witnesses who retract testimony of manumission. The commentator explains: The witnesses guarantee the full value of the slave because they caused the destruction of the owner's property (the slave) without any compensation. The wala (patronage) remains with the original manumittor, because manumission cannot transfer to the witnesses through this guarantee—the guarantee is purely financial compensation, not a transfer of status. The wording 'manumission' in the matn is absolute, covering both manumission without payment and manumission for a price. However, the commentator clarifies that the guarantee is for destruction of property, not for manumission per se, because the witnesses only destroyed their own property (by testifying) but the harm fell on the owner. The Lawgiver imposes this guarantee as a form of solace to the owner. The passage then gives a subcase: If witnesses testify that a man manumitted his slave for 500 dirhams (the slave's value being 1000), and the judge rules accordingly, then the witnesses retract, the owner may either claim the full 1000 from the witnesses (who then recover 500 from the slave) or directly claim the 500 from the slave (if he has it) and claim the remaining 500 from the witnesses. In either case, the wala remains with the original master. This is recorded in al-Muhit.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Financial liability or guarantee; the obligation to pay compensation for loss caused.
ولاء (wala'): Patronage right of the manumittor, including inheritance rights in the absence of blood relatives.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction; the act of a witness withdrawing his testimony after it has been given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two witnesses testify that a man manumitted his slave for 500 dirhams (slave value 1000), the judge rules, then they retract, what is the owner's option regarding compensation?
A) He may claim the full 1000 from the witnesses and they recover 500 from the slave.
B) He must claim only 500 from the witnesses.
C) He may claim the slave back from the witnesses.
D) He can only claim from the slave.

Key Takeaway
In retracted manumission testimony, witnesses guarantee full value but do not gain wala; liability is for property destruction, not for manumission.

Retraction of Witnesses: Manumission and Zina Cases
Case ruling on witness retraction after manumission and zina testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on financial liability when witnesses retract testimony in cases of manumission and zina leading to execution.

Core Idea
Each set of retracting witnesses pays for the specific harm they caused: value for property, diyah for life.

This lesson examines a complex scenario from al-Bazzaziyya where two sets of witnesses testify against a man, leading to contradictory outcomes: manumission and execution. After retraction, the judge must determine financial liability. The ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that witnesses are liable for the direct consequences of their testimony, and that compensation for different harms (property vs. life) is distinct and not duplicative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Witness Sets
There are two independent groups: two witnesses testify to manumission of a slave, four witnesses testify to zina while muhsan (married). Each set triggers a separate judgment: manumission and stoning.
The four witnesses for zina meet the required number for proof of zina.
2
Analyze the Consequences
The judge rules both the manumission and the stoning. The man is stoned to death. After the execution, all witnesses retract their testimony.
Retraction occurs after judgment and execution, so it does not reverse the judgment but triggers financial liability.
3
Determine Liability Per Set
The manumission witnesses must pay the value of the slave (since the master lost his property due to their testimony). The zina witnesses must pay the diyah (blood money) for the life of the master (since their testimony caused his execution). Both payments go to the master's estate.
The master's estate receives both payments because each compensates a different loss: property value and life value.
4
Consider the Master's Denial
If the master had denied the manumission, his denial is nullified by the judgment (since the judgment confirmed it). He is treated as non-existent for inheritance, meaning the diyah is not paid to him personally but goes to his estate (and is used to pay debts).
This prevents the master from benefiting from the manumission value if he denied the act, but the judgment overrides his claim.
5
Avoid Double Compensation
The value compensates for the loss of the slave (property). The diyah compensates for the loss of life (the master's life). These are two different losses, so there is no double compensation. The diyah belongs to the deceased's estate and must first be used to pay debts.
The text emphasizes: 'so there are not two compensations for one thing.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : شهدا على رجل بإعتاق عبده وأربعة أخر أنه زنى وهو محصن فحكم بالعتق والرجم ورجم ثم رجعوا فالقيمة على شهود العتق للمولى والدية على شهود الزنا للمولى أيضاً إن لم يكن له وراث آخر، والمولى إن كان جاحداً للعتق يمنع أخذ الدية لكن زعمه باطل بالحكم وصار كالمعدوم، ووجوب القيمة بدل المالية ووجوب الدية بدل النفس، ثم الدية للمقتول حتى تقضى بها ديونه فلا يلزم بدلان عن مبدل واحد اهـ.
And in al-Bazzaziyya: They testified against a man that he manumitted his slave and four others that he committed zina while muhsan, so he judged the manumission and the stoning, and he was stoned, then they retracted. So the value is upon the witnesses of manumission for the master, and the diyah upon the witnesses of zina for the master also if he has no other inheritor. And if the master was denying the manumission, he is prevented from taking the diyah, but his claim is void by the judgment and he becomes like non-existent. The obligation of the value is in place of the property, and the obligation of the diyah is in place of the life; then the diyah is for the killed person until his debts are paid from it, so there are not two compensations for one thing. End.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazzaziyya presents a complex case involving two sets of witnesses: two witnesses testify that a man manumitted his slave, and four witnesses testify that the same man committed zina while muhsan (married). The judge rules both the manumission and the stoning, and the man is stoned to death. Then all witnesses retract. The ruling: The manumission witnesses pay the value of the slave to the master (the deceased's estate), and the zina witnesses pay the diyah (blood money) for the slave's life to the master as well, provided the master has no other heir (other than the slave, who is now free). However, if the master himself had denied the manumission (i.e., he claimed he never manumitted), his denial is nullified by the judgment, and he is treated as non-existent for inheritance purposes (since the slave is now free and inherits? Actually careful: The master is the deceased? Wait: The man who was stoned is the owner of the slave. He was stoned to death. So his estate includes the slave? But the slave was manumitted by judgment. So the slave is free and does not inherit from the master? The master's heirs are considered. The text says 'if he has no other inheritor' meaning the master has no other heir besides the slave. Since the slave is free but cannot inherit from the master (because he was a slave at death? Actually the manumission was retroactive? The judgment establishes manumission from the time of testimony? Typically, manumission by judgment is effective from the time of testimony. So at the moment of the master's death, the slave was free, so he does not inherit. So the diyah goes to the master's other heirs or to the state. The commentator explains: The value for manumission witnesses compensates for the loss of the slave's property value. The diyah from zina witnesses compensates for the life of the master. The diyah is for the killed person (the master) and is used to pay his debts first; so there is no double compensation. The master's denial of manumission is irrelevant because the judgment has confirmed it.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for wrongful killing, here for executing a free man based on false testimony.
قيمة (qimah): Value, specifically the market value of the slave that the manumission witnesses must pay.
عتق (itq): Manumission, freeing a slave. Here, the subject of testimony leading to a judgment of manumission.
محصن (muhsan): A person who is married and free, rendering the punishment for zina as stoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two witnesses retracting manumission testimony and four retracting zina testimony against the same man, who pays the diyah?
A) The manumission witnesses pay the value, the zina witnesses pay the diyah.
B) Both sets of witnesses share the diyah equally.
C) Only the manumission witnesses pay everything.
D) The zina witnesses pay both value and diyah.

Key Takeaway
Each set of retracting witnesses compensates the specific harm caused by their testimony: property loss from manumission, life loss from zina.
